Distinction Between Entity and Accident with Rational Proof
Defines ʿayn and ʿaraḍ with rational proof and refutes opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fundamental distinction between an entity (ʿayn) and an accident (ʿaraḍ) based on the necessity of a locus for accidents, as known by self-evident reason, and the rejection of the Muʿtazilī position allowing accidents without a locus.

Core Idea
An accident cannot exist without a subject; self-evident reason proves it.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafī clarifies a key ontological distinction: an entity (ʿayn) can exist without a locus, whereas an accident (ʿaraḍ) cannot. He grounds this in the self-evident (badīhī) intellect, using the example of movement requiring a moving thing. He then critiques the Muʿtazila and Ibn al-Rāwandī for violating this rational principle. Let's examine the argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the author's definitions and argument.
Pay attention to the terms ʿayn and ʿaraḍ.
2
Identify the rational proof
Notice how the author uses self-evident reason (badīhah al-ʿaql) to prove that accidents require a locus. The example of movement is key.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Understand the refutation
The author criticizes the Muʿtazila and Ibn al-Rāwandī for allowing accidents without a locus, calling it impossible.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how rational self-evidence is used as a criterion in kalām to reject opposing views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرف ما هو المراد بهذه اللفظة ظهرت صحته وعلم به امتياز العرض من العين إذ العين ما يصح وجوده لا في محل، والعرض يستحيل وجوده لا في محل، إذ يعرف ببديهة العقل استحالة وجود حركة لا بمتحرك غير قائمة به وكذا في السواد والبياض وكل عرض. وكثير من المعتزلة وابن الراوندي اضطروا إلى ركوب هذا المحال وتجويز عرض حادث لا في محل، أداهم إليه أصولهم الفاسدة، وما أدى إلى المحال محال نبين ذلك عند انتهائنا إلى تلك المسائل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And when what is intended by this expression is known, its correctness becomes apparent, and by it the distinction between the accident (al-'arad) and the entity (al-'ayn) is known: for the entity is that whose existence is sound not in a locus, and the accident is that whose existence is impossible not in a locus, for it is known by the self-evidence of the intellect the impossibility of the existence of a movement without a moving thing, not subsisting in it, and likewise for blackness and whiteness and every accident. And many of the Mu'tazila and Ibn al-Rawandi were forced to ride this impossibility and to allow a temporal accident not in a locus, their corrupt principles led them to it, and that which leads to the impossible is impossible; we will clarify that when we reach those questions, God willing.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage clarifies the distinction between 'ayn (entity) and 'arad (accident) once the terminology is properly understood. The author states that an 'ayn is what can exist without a locus, while an 'arad cannot exist except in a locus. This is known by the self-evident (badihah) reason: for example, movement cannot exist without a moving thing to inhere in, and similarly blackness and whiteness require a subject. The author then criticizes the Mu'tazila and Ibn al-Rawandi who, due to their corrupt principles, allowed the possibility of a temporal accident existing without any locus. The author dismisses this as impossible and promises to refute it later.

Key Terms
عين
عرض
بديهة العقل
محل
ابن الراوندي
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some thinkers claim that accidents can exist without a locus.
Correction: The author argues this is impossible by self-evident reason, as movement requires a moving thing.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the proof that an accident cannot exist without a locus?
A) It is known by revelation
B) It is known by self-evident reason, e.g., movement requires a moving thing
C) It is known by consensus
D) It is known by analogy

Key Takeaway
The accident (ʿaraḍ) is inherently dependent on a subject, as proven by self-evident reason; the entity (ʿayn) is not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that accidents cannot exist without a subject. Consider your own fleeting emotions—anger, jealousy, excitement. Do you act as if these are independent realities, or do you recognize they depend on you as their subject? If you truly internalized that every accident needs a subject, how would it change your self-control and reliance on Allah?
?
The author condemns those who accept the impossible because of 'corrupt principles'. What principle in your life—pride, peer pressure, or convenience—has led you to accept something your own mind knows is false? Will you reject that and submit to the self-evident truth that every effect needs a cause?
?
This passage uses self-evident reason to confirm a fundamental truth about creation. Have you ever ignored the clear signs of Allah's oneness because of worldly attachments? What accident in your life are you treating as if it were an independent entity, when it is utterly dependent on its Creator?

Definitions of Body by Hisham and the Karramiyya
Nasafi presents Hisham's and the Karramiyya's definitions of body, implying anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the Karramiyya defined body (jism) and why their definitions led to anthropomorphism from the Maturidi perspective.

Core Idea
Defining Allah as a body reduces Him to creation — a serious error in tawhid.

In this passage, Nasafi presents two problematic definitions of body from Hisham ibn al-Hakam and the Karramiyya sect. These definitions, when applied to Allah, violate divine transcendence. Nasafi sets them up for refutation later. Let's examine the definitions and their theological consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Nasafi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two definitions by Hisham
The passage presents two definitions from Hisham: body as existent, and body as self-subsistent. Find both and note how they differ.
3
Observe the Karramiyya's adoption
See how the Karramiyya apply the second definition to Allah, concluding He is a body. Also note the alternative definition they sometimes give: body is that from which action is possible.
This is a key moment of anthropomorphism.
4
Consider Nasafi's response
Nasafi strongly repudiates these views and promises to refute them later. Ask yourself: why is this such a serious issue?
The answer lies in divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجسم فعند هشام بن الحكم هو الموجود إذ لا موجود عنده في الشاهد والغايب إلا الجسم وروي عنه أنه قال: إن الجسم هو القائم بالذات. ولهذا زعم أن جسم لما أنه موجود على الحد الأول، ولما أنه قائم بالذات على الحد الثاني. وتبعته الكرامية في الحد الثاني وزعمت أن الله تعالى جسم لأنه قائم بالذات. وربما يقولون: إن الجسم ما أمكن منه الفعل، والله تعالى أمكن منه الفعل فكان جسما تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرا - ونبين بطلان هذا كله إذا انتهينا إلى الكلام في إبطال قول المجسمة.
As for body (jism), according to Hisham ibn al-Hakam, it is the existent (al-mawjud), for there is no existent with him in the witnessed and the unseen except the body. And it is narrated from him that he said: 'The body is the self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat).' For this reason he claimed that [something] is a body insofar as it is existent according to the first definition, and insofar as it is self-subsistent according to the second definition. The Karramiyya followed him in the second definition and claimed that Allah the Exalted is a body because He is self-subsistent. And sometimes they say: 'The body is that from which action is possible, and Allah the Exalted, action is possible from Him, so He is a body.' Exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, greatly exalted. And we will demonstrate the invalidity of all this when we reach the discussion on refuting the statement of the corporealists (al-mujassima).
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of definitions. Nasafi first reports Hisham ibn al-Hakam's view that body (jism) is identical to existent (al-mawjud), as he denies any existent other than body. He also narrates that Hisham defined body as self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi al-dhat). Thus Hisham gives two definitions: body is existent, and body is self-subsistent. The Karramiyya adopted the second definition and applied it to Allah, concluding that Allah is a body because He is self-subsistent. Some Karramites also defined body as that from which action is possible, and since Allah acts, He is a body. Nasafi strongly repudiates these views as anthropomorphism and promises to refute them later in the book.

Key Terms
الجسم (al-jism)
الموجود (al-mawjud)
الشاهد والغايب (al-shahid wa al-gha'ib)
القائم بالذات (al-qa'im bi al-dhat)
المجسمة (al-mujassima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If Allah is self-subsistent (qa'im bi dhat), then He must be a body.
Correction: Self-subsistence does not entail having a body; it simply means existing without need of a place or substrate. The Karramiyya's error is conflating self-subsistence with corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which group followed Hisham ibn al-Hakam in defining body as self-subsistent and applied it to Allah?
A) The Ash'arites
B) The Mu'tazilites
C) The Karramiyya
D) The Mathematicians

Key Takeaway
Hisham's and the Karramiyya's definitions of body are rejected by Nasafi because they anthropomorphize Allah, reducing Him to the level of creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine a shape or form? What in this passage challenges you to purify your concept of Allah from any trace of corporealism?
?
Hisham and the Karramiyya made intellectual mistakes by defining Allah based on limited human categories. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you rely on human analogies? What concrete step will you take to replace that with the teaching of the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
This passage shows that wrong definitions can lead to serious error. How does your own understanding of Allah's attributes affect your worship? Would you be willing to study deeper to ensure you do not fall into even subtle anthropomorphism?

Refutation of Dirar and al-Najjar: Bodies as Accidents
Reports and refutes the view that bodies are composed solely of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deviant view that bodies are composed only of accidents and why the mainstream Maturidi position refutes it through the impossibility of accidents subsisting or composing.

Core Idea
Bodies are not mere collections of accidents; the indivisible substance (jawhar) is distinct from accidents.

Dirar ibn Amr and al-Husayn al-Najjar proposed a radical view that the world consists only of bodies and accidents, with bodies themselves being accidents aggregated together. This lesson presents their argument and the rational refutation based on the impossibility of accidents subsisting in themselves or composing other accidents. Understanding this refutation solidifies the standard substance-accident ontology in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on understanding the claims of Dirar and al-Najjar without analyzing yet.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Distinguish their view from mainstream
Notice that Dirar and al-Najjar affirm indivisible parts (like the mainstream) but call them bodies composed of accidents, not self-subsisting substances. Identify where the text states this.
The key difference: composition from accidents vs. a simple substance.
3
Identify the refutation
The author ends by stating that realizing the impossibility of accidents being composite or receiving accidents, and their inability to subsist in themselves, proves the view false. Pinpoint these three impossibilities.
Accidents cannot be composite because they are dependent on substances.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this refutation matters for the broader Maturidi cosmology. How does it preserve the distinction between substances (jawhar) and accidents?
This upholds the rational basis for tawhid and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا ضرار بن عمرو البصري رئيس الضرارية، والحسين بن محمد النجاري البصري رئيس النجارية أنكرا وجود ما وراء الأعراض في العالم، فإنهما زعما أن العالم أجسام وأعراض وأن الأجسام أعراض مجتمعة اجتمعت فاحتملت أعراضاً سواها. ثم الجسم عندهم يتركب من الأعراض التي لا يخلو الجسم منها أو من أضدادها كاللون والطعم والرائحة والحياة أو الموت، والطبائع الأربع فأما ما يخلو الجسم منها في الجملة كالعلم والقدرة والكلام وغير ذلك فليس من أبعاض الجسم، ثم عندهم أن الجسم الذي يقبل التجزؤ كل جزء منه جسم وإذا بلغ التجزؤ نهاية لا يحتمل التجزؤ بعد هذا لا بالفعل ولا بالوهم فذلك عندهم جسم، وهو الذي بينا أنه هو الجوهر عند عامة المتكلمين القائلين بعدم تجزؤ الجزء وإنما سموه جسما لأنه وإن لم يكن متركبا في نفسه من الأجزاء القائمة بذاتها فهو متركب من الأعراض التي مر ذكرها، فإذا هؤلاء ساعدوا القائلين بعدم تجزؤ الجزء غير أنهم قالوا : إنه مركب من الأعراض، وأحالوا وجود أبعاض الجسم متفرقة وسموا ما لا يتجزأ من الأجزاء جسما لتركبه من أعراض شتى، وبالوقوف على استحالة تركب الأعراض وقبولها أعراضا أخرى واستحالة بقائها في أنفسها يعرف بطلان هذا المذهب.
Likewise, Dirar ibn Amr al-Basri, the leader of the Dirariyya, and al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the leader of the Najjariyya, denied the existence of anything beyond accidents in the world. For they claimed that the world is bodies and accidents, and that bodies are accidents that have come together and thus bear other accidents. Then, according to them, the body is composed of accidents from which the body is never free or from their opposites, such as color, taste, smell, life or death, and the four natures. As for what the body is free from in general, like knowledge, power, speech, and so on, that is not among the parts of the body. Then, according to them, the body that admits division: every part of it is a body, and when division reaches an end such that it does not admit division after that, neither actually nor in estimation, that is, according to them, a body, and it is what we have explained to be the substance (jawhar) according to the generality of the mutakallimun who hold the indivisibility of the part. They only called it a body because, even if it is not composed in itself of self-subsisting parts, it is composed of the accidents mentioned earlier. So these people agreed with those who hold the indivisibility of the part, except that they said it is composed of accidents, and they deemed impossible the existence of separated parts of the body, and they called the indivisible part a body due to its composition from various accidents. By realizing the impossibility of accidents being composite and receiving other accidents, and the impossibility of their subsisting in themselves, the falseness of this doctrine is known.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage reports and refutes the positions of Dirar ibn Amr and al-Husayn al-Najjar, two sect leaders. They deny the existence of substance (jawhar) as something beyond accidents. They claim that the world consists only of bodies and accidents, and that bodies themselves are accidents that have combined together and can bear other accidents. According to them, a body is composed of essential accidents - those it cannot be without or their opposites, such as color, taste, smell, life/death, and the four primary qualities (hot, cold, wet, dry). Non-essential accidents like knowledge, power, and speech are not parts of the body. They further argue that when a body is divided until it reaches a point where it cannot be divided further, either actually or in estimation, that ultimate part is still called a body because it is composed of those accidents. This is similar to the mainstream mutakallimun's concept of jawhar (atom), but with the difference that they consider it composed of accidents. The author then states that by recognizing the impossibility of accidents being composite (since accidents cannot subsist in themselves or receive other accidents), the falseness of this view becomes clear.

Key Terms
ضرار بن عمرو (Dirar ibn Amr)
نجارية (Najjariyya)
أعراض (a'rad)
جوهر (jawhar)
تجزؤ (tajazzu')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Dirar and al-Najjar deny indivisible parts entirely.
Correction: In fact, they affirm indivisible parts but claim they are composed of accidents, not self-subsisting substances.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the key difference between the mainstream view of jawhar and the view of Dirar and al-Najjar?
A) Dirar and al-Najjar deny the existence of indivisible parts entirely.
B) Dirar and al-Najjar consider the indivisible part to be composed of accidents, not self-subsisting.
C) Dirar and al-Najjar believe bodies are composed of essential and non-essential accidents.
D) Dirar and al-Najjar affirm that accidents can subsist in themselves.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot compose a substance because they are non-self-subsistent and cannot receive other accidents, rendering the view of Dirar and al-Najjar rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals how a failure to understand the nature of accidents can lead to a false theology. Do you ever reduce your faith to mere external actions (accidents) while neglecting the substance of your heart (iman)? What would it take to ensure your deeds are rooted in a sound inner reality?
?
The refutation shows that accidents cannot support themselves—they depend entirely on a substance. In your life, do you rely on temporary 'accidents' (like health, wealth, or status) as if they were permanent? How can you shift your reliance to the unchanging substance—Allah—who alone is self-subsistent?
?
Dirar and al-Najjar denied anything beyond the observable accidents. Are there areas where you only see surface-level facts and ignore deeper truths—like the soul, the afterlife, or the unseen? Commit to one action this week that reminds you of the reality beyond the accidents.

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
8 min
Dirar and al-Najjar's View on Bodies and Its Refutation
Exposes the heretical view that bodies are composed of accidents and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the heretical view that bodies are mere composites of accidents and why it is impossible according to mainstream theologians.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot be composed of accidents; accidents depend on bodies, not vice versa.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents the doctrine of two early sectarian groups, the Dirariyya and Najjariyya, who denied any underlying substance beyond accidents. They claimed that bodies are nothing but collections of accidents that have aggregated. Before we examine the forceful refutation, let us first understand their position clearly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the authors being named and the structure of their argument.
This passage is dense; read twice if needed.
2
Identify the core claim
Pay attention to how Dirar and al-Najjar deny any reality beyond accidents. They reduce the world to bodies (as aggregated accidents) and accidents.
3
Understand their definition of body
They say body is composed of specific accidents like color, taste, smell, life/death, and the four natures. Other accidents are not part of the body's composition.
This is a key distinction from mainstream theology.
4
Note their view on the indivisible part
They agree with the existence of an indivisible part (what mainstream calls 'jawhar') but call it a body because it is composed of accidents.
5
Grasp the refutation
The refutation is based on three impossibilities: accidents cannot compose together, cannot receive other accidents, and cannot subsist in themselves. This shows the doctrine false.
This is the crucial logical point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا ضرار بن عمرو البصري رئيس الضرارية، والحسين بن محمد النجاري البصري رئيس النجارية أنكرا وجود ما وراء الأعراض في العالم، فإنهما زعما أن العالم أجسام وأعراض وأن الأجسام أعراض مجتمعة اجتمعت فاحتملت أعراضاً سواها. ثم الجسم عندهم يتركب من الأعراض التي لا يخلو الجسم منها أو من أضدادها كاللون والطعم والرائحة والحياة أو الموت، والطبائع الأربع فأما ما يخلو الجسم منها في الجملة كالعلم والقدرة والكلام وغير ذلك فليس من أبعاض الجسم، ثم عندهم أن الجسم الذي يقبل التجزؤ كل جزء منه جسم وإذا بلغ التجزؤ نهاية لا يحتمل التجزؤ بعد هذا لا بالفعل ولا بالوهم فذلك عندهم جسم، وهو الذي بينا أنه هو الجوهر عند عامة المتكلمين القائلين بعدم تجزؤ الجزء وإنما سموه جسما لأنه وإن لم يكن متركبا في نفسه من الأجزاء القائمة بذاتها فهو متركب من الأعراض التي مر ذكرها، فإذا هؤلاء ساعدوا القائلين بعدم تجزؤ الجزء غير أنهم قالوا : إنه مركب من الأعراض، وأحالوا وجود أبعاض الجسم متفرقة وسموا ما لا يتجزأ من الأجزاء جسما لتركبه من أعراض شتى، وبالوقوف على استحالة تركب الأعراض وقبولها أعراضا أخرى واستحالة بقائها في أنفسها يعرف بطلان هذا المذهب.
Likewise, Dirar ibn 'Amr al-Basri, the leader of the Dirariyya, and al-Husayn ibn Muhammad al-Najjar al-Basri, the leader of the Najjariyya, denied the existence of anything beyond accidents in the world. For they claimed that the world consists of bodies and accidents, and that bodies are accidents assembled together, which then came to bear other accidents. Then, according to them, the body is composed of those accidents from which the body is never free or from their opposites, such as color, taste, smell, life or death, and the four natures. As for what the body is entirely free from, such as knowledge, power, speech, and so on, that is not part of the body's parts. Then, according to them, a body that admits division: every part of it is a body; and when division reaches a limit where it no longer admits division, neither actually nor hypothetically, that is, according to them, a body. And it is what we have explained to be the substance (jawhar) according to the generality of theologians who affirm the indivisible part. They only call it a body because, although it is not composed in itself of self-subsistent parts, it is composed of the accidents mentioned. Thus, these people agreed with those who affirm the indivisible part, except that they said it is composed of accidents, and they deemed impossible the existence of the body's parts separate from each other, and they called that which is indivisible from the parts a body due to its composition from various accidents. By recognizing the impossibility of accidents composing and receiving other accidents, and the impossibility of their subsisting in themselves, the falsity of this doctrine is known.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the views of two heretical sects, the Dirariyya and the Najjariyya, regarding the nature of bodies. The author first states that these groups denied the existence of anything beyond accidents in the world. They claimed that the world consists only of bodies and accidents, and that bodies themselves are merely collections of accidents that have aggregated and then become capable of bearing other accidents. They defined the body as composed of certain accidents that it is never without, such as color, taste, smell, life or death, and the four primary qualities (heat, cold, moisture, dryness). Other accidents like knowledge, power, and speech are not part of the body's constitution. They held that a body that admits division is such that each part is itself a body; when division reaches an end where no further division is possible, that indivisible entity is also called a body. This corresponds to the substance (jawhar) of mainstream theologians who affirm the indivisible part. However, these sects differed from the mainstream by asserting that the indivisible unit is composed of accidents. They denied that the parts of a body could exist separately, and they called the indivisible unit a body because of its composition from various accidents. The refutation is then given: by understanding that accidents cannot compose together, cannot receive other accidents, and cannot subsist in themselves, the falsity of this doctrine becomes evident.

Key Terms
ضرار بن عمرو
النجاري
الأعراض
الجسم
الجوهر
عدم تجزؤ الجزء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bodies are merely collections of accidents that can subsist without a substance.
Correction: Accidents cannot compose together, cannot receive other accidents, and cannot subsist by themselves; therefore, bodies must have a substantial substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the fundamental error in Dirar and al-Najjar's view according to the author?
A) They denied the existence of the world entirely.
B) They claimed that accidents are self-subsistent and can compose together to form a body.
C) They rejected the indivisible part entirely.
D) They equated God with accidents.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are not composed of accidents; accidents depend on bodies, not the other way around.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced your understanding of faith to a collection of external actions (accidents) while neglecting the inner substance? What is one thing you can do today to focus on the core of your belief?
?
When you hear a theological argument that seems plausible but contradicts mainstream orthodoxy, do you accept it uncritically? How can you train yourself to examine such claims against established principles?
?
Reflect on the danger of building your worldview on a faulty foundation. In what areas of your life might you be relying on assumptions that, if false, would collapse your entire understanding? What step will you take to verify those foundations?

Three Views on the World's Origin
Imam Nasafi presents three views on the world's origin: created in both matter and form, eternal in both, or eternal in matter but created in form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three primary theological views on the world's origin as classified by Imam Nasafi.

Core Idea
The world's origin divides into three: fully created, fully eternal, or eternal matter with created form.

After establishing the definitions of eternal and originated, Imam Nasafi presents the three main positions on the world's origin. The first is the position of Ahl al-Haqq, that the world is originated in both matter and design. The second holds that it is eternal in both. The third holds that matter is eternal but design is originated. His discussion then begins to explore subdivisions among the second group.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the threefold division and the key terms.
The passage begins by stating the confinement of parts and definitions.
2
Identify the three groups
Find the three groups mentioned: Ahl al-Haqq (fully originated), Lam-yazaliyya (fully eternal), and the third group (eternal matter, originated design). Note the Arabic terms for each.
The third group is introduced but not fully developed.
3
Reflect on the 'people of truth'
Consider why the author calls the first group 'people of truth' (Ahl al-Ḥaqq). Also note the beginning of subdivisions among the second group.
The Lam-yazaliyya are not a single sect; they further divided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم حادث الطينة والصنعة خلافا لأقوام]
وإذا عرف انحصار جميع أجزاء العالم على ما بينا من الأقسام، وعرف القديم والمحدث فنقول : افترق الناس في أمر العالم على أقسام ثلاثة قال بعضهم وهم أهل الحق: إنه بجميع أقسامه وأجزائه محدث كائن بعد أن لم يكن، فكان حديث الطينة والصنعة. وقال بعض الناس : هو قديم الطينة والصنعة لا ابتداء لشيء منه البتة (۳) عرضا كان أو جوهرا بل هو بجميع أقسامه لم يزل ولا يزال، وهؤلاء يسمون لم يزليَّة. وقال بعضهم : العالم قديم الطينة .. حديث (4) الصنعة ثم افترق (٥) القائلون بقدم الطينة والصنعة جيمعا فقالت طائفة منهم : هو قديم لم يزل على ما نشاهده، ولا يزال أبدا كذلك و لا (٦) محدث له ولا مكون لاستغناء القديم عما يتعلق
[The world is originated in matter and design, contrary to some peoples]
And when the confinement of all parts of the world according to the divisions we explained is known, and the eternal and the originated are known, we say: People differed concerning the matter of the world into three divisions. Some of them—and they are the people of truth—said: It, in all its divisions and parts, is originated (muḥdath), coming into existence after it was not; thus it was originated in matter (ṭīna) and design (ṣunʿa). And some people said: It is eternal in matter and design; there is no beginning to any part of it at all (3), whether accident or substance; rather it, in all its divisions, has not ceased and will not cease; these are called the Lam-yazaliyya (Eternalists). And some of them said: The world is eternal in matter, originated (4) in design. Then those who said that it is eternal in both matter and design all differed; a group among them said: It is eternal, having not ceased as we observe it, and it will not cease likewise forever, and it has no (6) originated thing nor any constituent, because the eternal is independent of what relates to it...
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage presents the theological classification of views on the world's origin, framed after establishing the definitions of qadīm and muḥdath. The author states that people divided into three groups. The first group, 'the people of truth' (Ahl al-Ḥaqq), holds that the world in all its divisions and parts is originated (muḥdath)—it came into existence after non-existence—and thus it is originated in both matter (ṭīna) and design (ṣunʿa). The second group holds that the world is eternal in both matter and design, with no beginning to any part, whether accident or substance; it has always existed and will always exist. These are called the Lam-yazaliyya (the 'has-not-ceased' sect). The third group holds that the world is eternal in matter but originated in design. After this threefold division, the author begins to discuss further subdivisions among the second group (those who hold both matter and design are eternal). A subgroup among them says the world is eternal as we observe it, forever, with no originated or constituent, because the eternal is independent of anything related to it. The passage cuts off at this point, but the main threefold classification is complete.

Key Terms
حادث (originated)
قديم (eternal)
الطين (matter)
الصنعة (design)
لم يزليَّة (Eternalists)
محدث (originated thing)
مكون (constituent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Lam-yazaliyya are a single unified sect with no subdivisions.
Correction: The passage indicates that those who held both matter and design eternal further divided into subgroups, one of which held the world is eternal as observed.

Checkpoint
How many primary groups regarding the world's origin does the author mention before discussing the subdivisions?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The world's origin is a central theological issue, and Imam Nasafi classifies the views into three primary positions, with the true position being that the world is fully originated in both matter and design.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three views presented in this lesson does your heart actually lean toward when you consider your own attachment to material things? Do you live as if the world's matter and design are entirely dependent on the Creator, or do you sometimes act as if they are eternal and self-sufficient?
?
The passage identifies one view as the 'people of truth.' Are you willing to examine your life for evidence that you truly believe the world is fully originated—neither matter nor form existing without God's creation? What would change in your daily habits if you lived that belief consistently?

The Shubha for the Eternity of the World
Imam Nasafi presents the shubha of those who argue for the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and logical divisions of the shubha used by those who argue for the world's eternity.

Core Idea
A single shubha can be dismantled by tracing its logical divisions.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a detailed shubha from those who deny temporal creation. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing how it divides possibilities for the act of bringing into existence (ījād) and annihilation (i‘dām), leading to purported absurdities. Understanding this shubha is crucial for recognizing its flaws before refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is a single continuous shubha; treat it as an opponent's viewpoint.
2
Identify the three possibilities for ījād
Locate where the argument divides the act of bringing into existence into three options: being the essence of the eternal Originator, the essence of the originated, or a meaning other than both.
Each option is examined and deemed impossible, leading to a paradox.
3
Trace the argument for i‘dām
Notice the similar division for annihilation: either the annihilation is a separate meaning or identical to the world. Follow the reasoning that leads to infinite regress or contradiction.
The argument ends by claiming the world must be eternal.
4
Recognize the shubha as an opponent's argument
Understand that this is not Imam al-Nasafi's own view. He presents it to be refuted later. Note the attribution to groups like al-Najjāriyya, al-Ash‘arī, and al-Jāḥiẓ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده به، إذ هو المستغني بوجوده عن غيره، وشبهتهم أنهم قالوا: لو كان العالم محدثا لصار موجودا بإيجاد غيره إياه، ولو كان كذلك لكان الإيجاد إما ذات الموجد القديم، وإما ذات الموجد المحدث، وإما معنى هو غير هما، ولا جائز أن يكون ذات الموجد القديم لأنه يقتضي وجوده في الأزل لوجود ما هو إيجاده، ووجود الحادث في الأزل محال . وكذا تعلق وجود ما هو موجود في الأزل بالإيجاد محال، وإن كان الإيجاد ذات الموجد والإيجاد هو الموجد والإحداث هو المحدث فإذا لا تعلق لوجود العالم بغيره فكان قديما. وإن كان الإيجاد معنى وراء الموجد والعالم فهو إما أن يكون قائما بنفسه، وإما أن يكون قائما بغيره ومحال قيامه بنفسه لكونه صفة في نفسه وقيامه بغيره متنوع إلى قيامه بالموجد أو الموجد وقيامه بالموجد الحادث محال لما فيه من إخراج القديم من أن يكون موجدا إياه، قيامه بالموجد متنوع إلى كون الإيجاد قديما وكونه حديثا، فإن كان حديثا فالقديم ليس بمحل الحوادث، وإن كان قديما فقدمه يقتضي قدم الموجد الحادث، والقديم يستحيل تعلق وجوده بغيره، وإذا كان كونه محدثا ينقسم إلى هذه الأقسام وهي باطلة دل أن القول بحدوث العالم أو حدوث شيء من أجزائه باطل وكذا لو جاز عدم العالم إما أن كان إعدام القديم إياه معنى غيره من أجل وجوده ينعدم، وإما أن كان إعدام العالم هو هو، فإن كان غيره، فإن كان الإعدام قديما فيقتضي انعدامه من الأصل ولا يتصور وجوده، وحين رأيناه موجودا كان الحس والبيان مبطلا كون الإعدام قديما، وإن كان الإعدام حديثا إما أن كان هو عين العالم، وإما أن كان غيره فإذا فسد أن يعدم جميع العالم حتى لا يبقى حادث إذ هذا الذي به عدم العالم حادث وكل حادث فهو من أجزاء العالم، ولو أراد إعدام هذا لابد من حادث آخر هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية، فإن كان إعدام العالم هو عين العالم فالعالم إذا معدوم لوجود إعدامه، موجود لحصوله وثبوته في الحس، فهو إذا معدوم موجود ، ويكون معنى قولنا: وجد العالم أي عدم وكذا إيجاد العالم إذا كان هو العالم عند أكثركم على ما ذهبت إليه النجارية والأشعري وأكثر المعتزلة، فإن إيجاد العالم هو هو وإعدامه هو هو، فإذا إيجاده إعدامه وإعدامه إيجاده، فمتى أوجده الموجد فقد أعدمه ومتى أعدمه فقد أوجده، فكان موجودا عند إعدامه معدوما عند إيجاده، وهذا خلف من القول، بل هو دخول في السوفسطائية، ولهذا جعل الجاحظ القول بعدم الأجسام مستحيلا، وأخرج إعدامها عن مقدور الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك فقد ثبت قدم العالم بجميع أجزائه وتحقق استحالة عدمه بجميع أجزائه أيضا . هذا هو شبهة هذه الطائفة على الخصوص.
Its existence through itself, for it is self-sufficient in its existence from others. And their shubha (sophism) is that they said: If the world were temporally originated, it would become existent by the bringing into existence (ījād) of another. And if that were so, then the bringing into existence would be either the essence (dhāt) of the eternal originator (mūjid), or the essence of the originated originator, or a meaning (ma‘nā) other than the two. It is not permissible that it be the essence of the eternal originator because it would require His existence in pre-eternity for the existence of what is His bringing into existence, and the existence of the originated in pre-eternity is impossible. Likewise, the attachment of the existence of what exists in pre-eternity to bringing into existence is impossible. And if the bringing into existence is the essence of the originator, and the originator [itself] is the bringing into existence, and the originating (iḥdāth) is the originator (muḥdith), then there is no attachment of the world's existence to another, so it would be eternal. And if the bringing into existence is a meaning beyond the originator and the world, then it is either self-subsistent or subsistent in another. Self-subsistence is impossible for it because it is an attribute (ṣifa) in itself. Its subsistence in another varies between its subsistence in the originator or the originated. Its subsistence in the originated originator is impossible because it would remove the eternal from being its originator. Its subsistence in the originator varies between the bringing into existence being eternal or originated. If it is originated, then the eternal is not a locus for originated things. If it is eternal, then its eternity requires the eternity of the originated originator, and the eternal's existence cannot be attached to another. And if its being originated is divided into these divisions, and they are invalid, it indicates that the statement of the temporal origination of the world or any of its parts is invalid. Likewise, if the non-existence (ʿadam) of the world is possible, either the annihilation (i‘dām) of the eternal of it is a meaning other than it due to which it becomes non-existent, or the annihilation of the world is itself [the world]. If it is other, then if the annihilation is eternal, it requires its non-existence from the origin and its existence cannot be conceived. And when we see it existent, sensory perception and demonstration invalidate the annihilation being eternal. If the annihilation is originated, then it is either identical to the world or other. If other, then it is impossible to annihilate the entire world so that no originated remains, because that by which the world becomes non-existent is originated, and every originated is part of the world. If one intends to annihilate this, there must be another originated, and so on ad infinitum. If the annihilation of the world is identical to the world, then the world is non-existent due to the existence of its annihilation, and existent due to its attainment and establishment in sensory perception. So it is non-existent and existent. And the meaning of our saying 'the world came to be' would be 'it became non-existent'. Likewise, the bringing into existence of the world, if it is the world itself according to most of you, as held by al-Najjāriyya, al-Ash‘arī, and most of the Mu‘tazila, then its bringing into existence is itself and its annihilation is itself. So its bringing into existence is its annihilation and its annihilation is its bringing into existence. Whenever the originator brings it into existence, He annihilates it, and whenever He annihilates it, He brings it into existence. So it would be existent at its annihilation and non-existent at its bringing into existence. This is a contradiction in statement; rather it is an entry into sophistry (sūfisṭāʾiyya). For this reason, al-Jāḥiẓ deemed the statement of the non-existence of bodies impossible and excluded their annihilation from the power of Allah. If that is so, then the eternity of the world in all its parts is established, and the impossibility of its non-existence in all its parts is also realized. This is the shubha of this particular group.
Source pages p. 200

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of a sophistical argument (shubha) used by those who deny the temporal creation of the world and assert its eternity. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, recounts the argument in full before refuting it. The shubha begins by positing that if the world were originated, it would require an act of bringing into existence (ījād) by another. It then divides the possibilities for this act: (1) it could be the essence of the eternal Originator, (2) the essence of the originated being itself, or (3) a separate meaning. Each possibility is examined and shown to lead to absurdities. For example, if the act were the essence of the eternal Originator, then the originated would have to exist in pre-eternity, which is impossible. If it were the essence of the originated, then the world would have no external cause and thus be eternal. If it were a separate meaning subsisting in the Originator, it would either be eternal (making the originated eternal) or originated (implying change in God). All divisions are invalidated. The argument then turns to annihilation (i‘dām). It divides possibilities similarly, leading to an infinite regress or the conclusion that existence and non-existence coincide, which is sophistical. The shubha ends by attributing this position to various groups and concluding that the world is eternal in all its parts. The passage is careful to label this as a shubha, not the author's own view.

إيجاد
إعدام
قديم
محدث
موجد
ذات
معنى
قيام
سوفسطائية

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Readers may mistakenly think this passage represents Imam al-Nasafi's own view on the creation of the world.
Correction: This passage is a presentation of an opponent's argument (shubha) that will be refuted. Imam al-Nasafi does not endorse the eternity of the world.

Checkpoint
According to the shubha presented, what is the first division made regarding the act of bringing into existence (ījād) if the world were originated?
A) It could be eternal or originated.
B) It could be the essence of the eternal Originator, the essence of the originated, or a meaning other than both.
C) It could be self-subsistent or subsistent in another.
D) It could be identical to the world or separate.

Key Takeaway
The shubha attempts to prove the world's eternity by dividing the act of creation into impossible alternatives, but it is a sophistical argument to be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you encounter arguments that sound convincing but are built on false premises? This shubha is a reminder that even a seemingly logical case can be deeply flawed. In your own beliefs, where might you be relying on a 'shubha' instead of sound reasoning? What would it take to honestly examine the foundations of your convictions?
?
The shubha leads to a contradiction if one assumes the world is created. But we know from revelation that Allah created the world. Does your certainty in this truth come from intellectual argument or from trust in divine news? If an argument like this shook you, would your faith stand? What are you doing to strengthen both your intellectual certainty and your heart's surrender?
?
Al-Jāḥiẓ denied the possibility of bodies being annihilated, and this shubha uses that to argue for eternity. Yet we know Allah is capable of everything. Are there areas of your life where you limit Allah's power based on your own reasoning? This lesson exposes the danger of making human logic a judge over divine capability. Where do you need to submit your intellect to Allah's absolute power?

The Argument for an Eternal Created World
Imam Nasafi presents a second group's argument for the eternity of the world based on causality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second shubha that argues the world is manufactured yet eternal due to the eternity of its cause.

Core Idea
If the cause is eternal, the effect must be eternal.

Imam Nasafi continues his exposition of objections to the creation of the world. After presenting those who deny a Creator, he now turns to a second group that acknowledges a Creator but claims the world is eternal. Their argument hinges on a philosophical notion of causality: the effect cannot be separated from its cause. This passage presents their reasoning, though it is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that it presents a second objection (shubha) from a group that accepts a Creator but claims the world is eternal.
The passage cuts off mid-sentence at 'and His existence'.
2
Identify the two claims
The group first says the world is manufactured (maṣnū‘) and has a maker, based on evidence that it depends on another. Then they claim it is eternal (qadīm) due to the eternity of its maker.
3
Understand the causal logic
They use the philosophical term 'cause' (ʿilla) and 'effect' (ma‘lūl). The effect cannot separate from the cause nor be posterior to it. If the cause is eternal, the effect must also be eternal.
This is a key misunderstanding that Islamic theologians reject.
4
Note the incompleteness
The text ends abruptly. The original likely continues with a refutation. For now, focus on understanding the argument as presented.
This shubha will be answered later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت طائفة أخرى منهم بل العالم مصنوع وله صانع لقيام دلالة كونه بغيره لا بنفسه غير أنه قديم لقدم صانعه لأن صانعه علة لكونه، والمعلول لا يفارق العلة ولا يتأخر عنه، وبعضهم قالوا سبب وجود العالم وجود البارئ، ووجوده
And another group among them said: Rather the world is manufactured (maṣnū‘) and has a maker (ṣāni‘) due to the establishment of evidence that it is through another not through itself, but it is eternal due to the eternity of its maker, because its maker is the cause (ʿilla) of its existence, and the effect (ma‘lūl) does not separate from the cause nor is it posterior to it. And some of them said: The cause of the world's existence is the existence of the Creator (al-Bāri’), and His existence...
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents a second shubha from a group that does not deny the existence of a Creator but argues for the eternity of the world. They admit that the world is manufactured and has a maker, based on evidence that it is dependent on another. However, they claim the world is eternal because its maker is eternal. The reasoning is that the maker is the cause (ʿilla) of the world's existence, and the effect (ma‘lūl) cannot be separated from the cause nor be posterior to it in time. Some among them specify that the cause of the world's existence is the very existence of the Creator (al-Bāri’). The text cuts off mid-sentence at 'and His existence', so the rest of the argument is missing. This shubha is part of the author's exposition of false arguments for the eternity of the world, which he will subsequently refute.

Key Terms

علة
معلول
بارئ
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if something has a cause, and the cause is eternal, then the effect must be eternal as well.
Correction: Islamic theologians reject this because the Creator's will is free and not a necessary cause; the effect (world) is temporally originated even though the cause is eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given by this second group for the world being eternal?
A) The world has no maker.
B) The world is self-subsistent.
C) The maker is eternal and the effect cannot be separate from the cause.
D) The world was created out of pre-existing matter.

Key Takeaway
This shubha claims that the world is eternal because its cause (the Creator) is eternal, and cause and effect cannot be separated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

What does that reveal about the strength of your own conviction in the Islamic belief of the world's temporal origin?
?
The group in this passage conceded that the world is manufactured but denied its temporality. Are there areas in your own faith where you accept a truth (like God's existence) but still allow philosophical reasoning to lead you to wrong conclusions about His attributes? What is one belief you hold that you have never critically examined against this standard?

Refutation of the Sophism of Annihilation Eternality
The author records and refutes a sophistical argument for the world's eternity based on identity of annihilation and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sophistical argument that claims the world's annihilation is identical to its existence, and why the author refutes it as contradictory sophistry.

Core Idea
Claiming annihilation equals existence leads to absurdity and undermines rational theology.

The author records a sophistical argument used by some to assert the world's eternity. The argument attempts to show that if annihilation is the same as the world itself, then contradictions arise. This passage exposes the fallacy and shows why it cannot be accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the argument's flow become clear.
The author is presenting a sophism, not endorsing it.
2
Identify the premises of the argument
Look for the initial claim about every originated thing, the infinite regress, and the key premise that annihilation is identical to the world.
These are the building blocks of the sophism.
3
Understand the consequences drawn
The author shows that if annihilation equals existence, then paradoxical statements follow: the world would be both existent and non-existent, and creation would be destruction.
This is meant to expose the absurdity.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Note how the author labels the argument as contradictory and sophistical. Also note al-Jāḥiẓ's position and the conclusion that this is not a valid proof.
The author uses the argument to show its own failure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل حادث فهو من أجزاء العالم، ولو أراد إعدام هذا لابد من حادث آخر هكذا إلى ما لا نهاية، فإن كان إعدام العالم هو عين العالم فالعالم إذا معدوم لوجود إعدامه، موجود لحصوله وثبوته في الحس، فهو إذا معدوم موجود ، ويكون معنى قولنا: وجد العالم أي عدم وكذا إيجاد العالم إذا كان هو العالم عند أكثركم على ما ذهبت إليه النجارية والأشعري وأكثر المعتزلة، فإن إيجاد العالم هو هو وإعدامه هو هو، فإذا إيجاده إعدامه وإعدامه إيجاده، فمتى أوجده الموجد فقد أعدمه ومتى أعدمه فقد أوجده، فكان موجودا عند إعدامه معدوما عند إيجاده، وهذا خلف من القول، بل هو دخول في السوفسطائية، ولهذا جعل الجاحظ القول بعدم الأجسام مستحيلا، وأخرج إعدامها عن مقدور الله تعالى، وإذا كان كذلك فقد ثبت قدم العالم بجميع أجزائه وتحقق استحالة عدمه بجميع أجزائه أيضا . هذا هو شبهة هذه الطائفة على الخصوص.
And every originated thing is part of the world, and if He willed to annihilate this, another originated thing would be necessary, and so on to infinity. For if the annihilation of the world is the essence of the world itself, then the world is non-existent because its annihilation exists, and it is existent because it is attained and established in sensation. It is therefore non-existent and existent. And the meaning of our saying 'the world exists' would be 'it does not exist', and likewise the bringing into existence of the world, if it is the world itself according to most of you as held by al-Najjāriyya, al-Ashʿarī, and most of the Muʿtazila, for the bringing into existence of the world is identical to it and its annihilation is identical to it. Thus its bringing into existence is its annihilation, and its annihilation is its bringing into existence. So whenever the Bringer into existence brings it into existence, He annihilates it, and whenever He annihilates it, He brings it into existence. Thus it would be existent at the time of its annihilation and non-existent at the time of its bringing into existence. This is contradictory speech; rather it is an entry into sophistry. For this reason al-Jāḥiẓ considered the statement of the non-existence of bodies impossible and excluded their annihilation from the power of God Almighty. And if this is so, then the eternity of the world with all its parts is established and the impossibility of its non-existence with all its parts is also realized. This is the sophism of this particular sect.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes a sophistical argument used by a specific group to assert the eternity of the world. The argument begins by assuming that every originated thing is part of the world, so the annihilation of the world would require another originated event, leading to an infinite regress. Then the claim is made that if the annihilation of the world is identical to the world itself (i.e., essences of existence and non-existence are the same), then contradictions follow: the world would be both existent and non-existent simultaneously. The author notes that this view is held by the Najjariyya, Ashʿari, and most Muʿtazila according to the argument's premises. He points out the absurd consequence that bringing into existence and annihilation would be the same act, so the world would exist when annihilated and be non-existent when created. This, he says, is contradictory and amounts to sophistry. He cites al-Jāḥiẓ as using this reasoning to deny the possibility of bodies ceasing to exist, thus removing their annihilation from God's power. The passage concludes that if this sophism were accepted, it would imply the world is eternal and cannot cease to exist. However, the author clearly presents it as a fallacy, not as a valid proof.

شبهة
سوفسطائية
النجارية
الجاحظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the author is endorsing the argument and its conclusion that the world is eternal.
Correction: The author is reporting the argument to refute it, calling it 'contradictory speech' and 'entry into sophistry'. He does not accept it as valid.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the argument that annihilation of the world is identical to the world itself?
A) It proves the world is eternal.
B) It is a contradictory sophism that leads to absurdity.
C) It is a valid rational proof accepted by most theologians.
D) It shows that God cannot annihilate the world.

Key Takeaway
The sophistical argument that equates annihilation with existence is self-contradictory and cannot be used to prove the world's eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself using comfortable reasoning to justify a belief or action that deep down you know is inconsistent with the truth, what will you do to expose that sophistry in your own heart?
?
This passage shows that equating God's creation with annihilation leads to absurdity. Have you ever, even subtly, attributed to God a quality that diminishes His perfection? What will you do to guard His attributes from your own flawed logic?
?
The author labels the argument as 'sophistry'—a deception that appears logical. In your prayer and daily life, do you ever deceive yourself that you are drawing closer to God while actually building arguments to avoid obedience? What specific step will you take today to break that pattern?

The World Originated from Eternal Matter: The Hyle Theory
The author enumerates three sub-variants of the hyle theory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the heretical view that the world is originated but from an eternal origin (hyle) and its three sub-variants, and why kalam theologians refute it.

Core Idea
Some claimed the world emerged from an eternal, formless matter they described like God—but without a Creator.

In this passage, the author reports a deviant view: the world is not created ex nihilo but from an eternal principle called hyle. This view has several sub-branches. The author presents them so that they may be refuted later. Pay attention to how the hyle is described with divine attributes—a clear misappropriation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation without pausing. Get the overall structure of the argument.
2
Identify the three positions within the hyle theory
The passage breaks into three subgroups. Find each one: (1) accidents occur without a maker, (2) a maker brings about accidents, (3) the maker is the four natures. This is a typical doxographical enumeration.
3
Notice the description of hyle
Observe how the proponents describe hyle using attributes that belong to God alone (no quantity, no quality, free from deficiency). This is a key point for refutation.
The author is reporting, not endorsing.
4
Consider why this matters
Think about how this view undercuts divine transcendence and creative power. It makes the world eternal in its matter, denying true creation.
This is a major heresy in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالت جماعة منهم: العالم حادث لكنه من أصل قديم له، واختلف هؤلاء في الأصل القديم، منهم من قال : هو الهيولى، ويصفون الهيولى بما يصف أهل التوحيد الله تعالى، أنه موجود ليس له كمية ولا كيفية ولم يقترن به شيء من سمات الحدوث والنقص ثم يقولون حلت به الصنعة واعترضت فيه الأعراض فحدث منه العالم ثم من أصحاب الهيولى من يزعم أن حدوث الأعراض لم يكن بصانع إذ لا صانع ولا قديم غير الهيولى ومنهم من زعم أن صانع العالم هو الذي أحدث منه العالم بإحداث الصنعة والأعراض فيه. ومنهم من زعم أن صانع العالم هو الطبائع الأربع البسيطة أعنى الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة اليبوسة فتركب العالم من هذه الأربعة القديمة، وينسب بعض الناس هذا القول إلى جالنيوس، وأنكر كثير من
And a group among them said: The world is originated, but from an eternal origin belonging to it. And they differed concerning the eternal origin. Among them was one who said: It is the hyle (al-hayūlā). And they describe the hyle with what the people of tawḥīd describe God Almighty: that it is existent, having no quantity nor quality, and no attributes of origination and deficiency are attached to it. Then they say: craftsmanship settled in it, and accidents entered into it, so the world originated from it. Then among the proponents of the hyle are those who claim that the occurrence of accidents was not due to a maker, because there is no maker nor eternal other than the hyle. And among them are those who claimed that the maker of the world is the one who originated the world from it by bringing about craftsmanship and accidents in it. And among them are those who claimed that the maker of the world is the four simple natures, namely heat, cold, moisture, and dryness; so the world was composed from these four eternal [principles]. Some people attribute this statement to Galen; and many [denied?]
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents the views of a group who maintain that the world is originated (ḥādith) but from an eternal origin (aṣl qadīm). The author then outlines three subdivisions. The first holds that this eternal origin is hylic matter (al-hayūlā), which is described in terms reminiscent of how the people of tawḥīd describe God: it is existent with no quantity, no quality, and free from any attributes of origination or deficiency. The process is that 'craftsmanship' (al-ṣun'a) enters into the hyle, and accidents (aʿrāḍ) intervene, resulting in the world. Within this group, there are three further positions: (1) Some deny any maker for the accidents, asserting that nothing else is eternal besides the hyle itself. (2) Others claim that there is a maker (God) who originated the world from the hyle by introducing craftsmanship and accidents. (3) Yet others claim the maker is not a transcendent deity but the four simple natures (heat, cold, moisture, dryness), which are eternal, and the world is composed from them. This last view is attributed to Galen. The passage cuts off mid-sentence: 'and many [denied?]' likely indicating that many rejected this attribution to Galen.

Key Terms
هيولى
أصل قديم
صنعة
أعراض
طبائع أربع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The description of hyle in divine terms is the author's own theological position.
Correction: The author is merely reporting a heretical view; he does not endorse it. He uses this description to highlight the error of attributing divine qualities to matter.

Checkpoint
What do the proponents of the hyle (al-hayūlā) claim about the eternal origin?
A) It is God Himself.
B) It is a formless matter with no quantity or quality.
C) It is composed of the four natures.
D) It was created by the four simple natures.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows how some sought to keep an eternal element in creation, misusing divine attributes to describe matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The hyle proponents described matter with attributes that belong only to Allah (existence without quantity or quality). Is there anything in your life—a person, a possession, a pursuit—that you subconsciously treat as if it has a kind of eternal or self-sufficient quality? How does this passage expose that tendency? What concrete step will you take today to strip that thing of any divine-like status in your heart?
?
Notice that even among those who believed in eternal matter, there was deep disagreement about whether a maker exists. This shows that reason alone cannot settle the ultimate questions about origins. Where do you find yourself relying on your own reasoning rather than on revelation when thinking about God and creation? Will you commit to testing every assumption about Allah against the Qur’an and Sunnah starting this week?
?
If someone told you that the universe came from an eternal material source, you might feel that it diminishes the need for God. The passage records that some hyle proponents still affirmed a Creator. What does that teach you about the human impulse to preserve some role for a divine being even within a materialist framework? Examine your own heart: have you ever tried to 'fit' God into a worldview that actually denies His absolute sovereignty? How will you repent from that and submit fully to the tawhid of Allah?

Arguments for the Eternity of the World
Nasafi enumerates various doctrines denying creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various arguments used by those who deny the temporal creation of the world, whether by asserting its pre-eternity or its origination from an eternal principle.

Core Idea
False arguments for an eternal world misuse divine attributes like generosity, knowledge, wisdom, and power.

This passage by Imam Nasafi presents a comprehensive survey of the positions taken by those who claim that the world is either eternal or originated from an eternal source. These are not his own views but rather the doctrines he will refute after establishing the world's creation ex nihilo. Understanding these arguments is crucial because they represent the intellectual challenges that Islamic theology must address.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main categories
Notice that Nasafi first lists those who claim the entire world is pre-eternal, then those who say it is originated from an eternal origin.
This division is key to understanding the structure of the passage.
2
Examine the arguments from divine attributes
Read how each attribute (generosity, knowledge, wisdom, power) is used to argue that the world must be eternal. Ask yourself if these attributes necessarily require an eternal effect.
3
Understand the hyle theories
Some say the world comes from an eternal hyle (prime matter), with differing views on whether a maker exists. Note how they describe hyle similar to how Muslims describe God.
This is a clear deviation from tawhid.
4
Notice the final grouping and the common doubt
Others cite four natures or four elements as the origin. All share the doubt that existence from nothing is impossible. See how this doubt underpins their arguments.
The doubt is: existence from nothing is impossible, so there must be an eternal substrate.
5
Prepare for refutation
Realize that these are the claims Nasafi will refute after proving the world's creation. Keep in mind that these are reported as false doctrines, not accepted beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أزلى لاستحالة البخل عليه في الأزل فيقتضي قدم جوده قدم ما يتعلق وجوده بجوده. وهؤلاء يقولون في تسبيحهم الله تعالى: يا من جوده سبب وجود كل موجود. وبعضهم يقولون: كان الباري جل وعلا في الأزل عالما بالعالم موجودا لأنه علمه قديم لاستحالة الجهل عليه، وقدم علمه يقتضي قدم معلومه. ومنهم من قال: القديم لم يزل حكيما، فاقتضى ذلك وجود حكمته وهو العالم في الأزل. وقال بعضهم: إن الله تعالى كان في الأزل قادرا على الفعل، ولا مانع له عن الفعل فلا معنى لتأخر فعله. هذا هو مقالة القائلين بكون العالم بجميع أجزائه قديما. وقالت جماعة منهم: العالم حادث لكنه من أصل قديم له، واختلف هؤلاء في الأصل القديم. منهم من قال: هو الهيولى، ويصفون الهيولى بما يصف أهل التوحيد الله تعالى، أنه موجود ليس له كمية ولا كيفية ولم يقترن به شيء من سمات الحدوث والنقص. ثم يقولون حلت به الصنعة واعترضت فيه الأعراض فحدث منه العالم. ثم من أصحاب الهيولى من يزعم أن حدوث الأعراض لم يكن بصانع إذ لا صانع ولا قديم غير الهيولى. ومنهم من زعم أن صانع العالم هو الذي أحدث منه العالم بإحداث الصنعة والأعراض فيه. ومنهم من زعم أن صانع العالم هو الطبائع الأربع البسيطة أعني الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة فتركب العالم من هذه الأربعة القديمة، وينسب بعض الناس هذا القول إلى جالينوس، وأنكر كثير من الناس نسبة هذا القول إليه وقالوا: إنه كان يقول بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع القديم، والمذهب المشهور عن جالينوس التوقف في القول بحدوث العالم وقدمه، وثبوت الصانع وعدمه، وفي ثبوت المعاد ونفيه. ومنهم من زعم أن أصل العالم الطبائع المركبة، وهي النار والماء والأرض والهواء ويسمونها العناصر، وقد يسمونها الإسفنطسات. وشبهة هؤلاء كلهم أن وجود الشيء لا من شيء محال وكل صانع يصنع في محل إلا أن يوجد المحل أصلا. هذه عمدة أقاويل هؤلاء، ولهم أقاويل مختلفة جارية مجرى الفروع لهذه فأعرضنا عن ذكرها لخروجها عما هو شرط كتابنا هذا، ونبين فساد هذه الأقاويل بعد إقامة الدليل على كون العالم بجميع أقسامه محدثا إن شاء الله تعالى.
Eternal: due to the impossibility of stinginess upon Him in pre-eternity, so the pre-eternity of His generosity necessitates the pre-eternity of that whose existence is connected with His generosity. And these people say in their glorification of God Almighty: 'O He whose generosity is the cause of every existent.' And some of them say: The Creator, Great and Exalted, was in pre-eternity knowing the existing world because His knowledge is pre-eternal due to the impossibility of ignorance upon Him, and the pre-eternity of His knowledge necessitates the pre-eternity of its object. And among them is one who said: The Pre-eternal was always wise, so that necessitated the existence of His wisdom, and He is the world in pre-eternity. And some of them said: God Almighty was in pre-eternity powerful over action, and there is no hindrance to Him from action, so there is no meaning to the delay of His action. This is the statement of those who claim that the world in all its parts is pre-eternal. And a group among them said: The world is originated, but from a pre-eternal origin of it, and these differed concerning the pre-eternal origin. Among them is one who said: It is hyle, and they describe hyle with what the people of tawhid describe God Almighty: that it is existent having neither quantity nor quality, and no attribute of temporality or defect is associated with it. Then they say: Artifice settled in it, and accidents became manifest in it, so the world came to be from it. Then, among the proponents of hyle, some claim that the origination of accidents was not from a maker, for there is no maker nor pre-eternal other than hyle. And among them is one who claimed that the Maker of the world is the one who originated the world from it by causing artifice and accidents to occur in it. And among them is one who claimed that the Maker of the world is the four simple natures, that is, heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, so the world is composed from these four pre-eternal things, and some people attribute this statement to Galen, but many people deny attributing this statement to him, saying: He used to say that the world is originated and the existence of the Pre-eternal Maker, and the well-known position of Galen is suspension of judgment concerning the statement of the world's origination and its pre-eternity, the existence of the Maker and His nonexistence, and concerning the establishment of the Resurrection and its negation. And among them is one who claimed that the origin of the world is the composite natures, which are fire, water, earth, and air, and they call them the elements, and sometimes they call them the istaqsat. And the doubt of all these is that the existence of a thing from nothing is impossible, and every maker makes only in a substrate unless the substrate exists as an origin. These are the main statements of these, and they have other various statements running as branches of these, so we refrain from mentioning them because they are outside what is required by this book of ours, and we will explain the corruption of these statements after establishing the proof that the world in all its parts is originated, God willing.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a classification and exposition of the various false doctrines concerning the temporal origin of the world. Nasafi lists the arguments of those who assert the world's pre-eternity (qidam) or its origination from an eternal principle. He proceeds in order: first, those who argue from God's generosity (jood), knowledge ('ilm), wisdom (hikma), and power (qudra) to the necessity of an eternal effect. Then he mentions those who say the world is originated but from an eternal substratum, and among them, the proponents of hyle (hayula) who differ on whether a Maker exists or not. Then he mentions the four natures and the attribution to Galen, and the four elements. He concludes by saying these are the main arguments, and he will refute them after proving the temporal initiation of the world.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world must be eternal because God's attributes such as generosity, knowledge, wisdom, and power are eternal and thus require an eternal effect.
Correction: God's attributes are eternal but they do not necessitate an eternal creation; creation is contingent upon God's will, which is not compelled by the attributes.

D) Those who say the origin is the four composite natures (elements)

Key Takeaway
This passage outlines the main positions that deny the createdness of the world, all of which will be refuted by Imam Nasafi later.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hyle theorists attributed to matter what only God deserves. What created thing—such as wealth, health, or a relationship—do you treat as necessary or eternal, as if it were self-sufficient? Identify one and repent by actively relying on God alone for it.

Nasafi lists these false doctrines without yet refuting them. Are any of these doubts secretly lingering in your heart, such as 'maybe the world is eternal' or 'matter might be pre-eternal'? Confess this to yourself and resolve to study the proofs for creation to fortify your faith.

Survey of Eternal World Claims
Nasafi surveys two eternalist views and their shared doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main opinions of those who assert the eternity of the world and the common doubt they share.

Core Idea
Eternalists agree: something cannot come from nothing.

Before establishing the proof that the world is created, Imam Nasafi surveys the views of those who claim it is eternal. He mentions two distinct positions: one attributed to Galen and another held by those who believe the four elements are the original principles. Both share a fundamental doubt that shapes their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first opinion
Read the beginning of the passage and note what is attributed to an unnamed person (likely Galen) regarding the world and the Maker.
The text says 'النسبة هذا القول إليه' meaning people attribute this saying to him.
2
Examine Galen's position
Find the statement about Galen's suspension of judgment and list the three matters he suspends judgment on.
Galen suspends judgment on the world's creation/eternity, the Maker, and the resurrection.
3
Understand the element theory
Read about those who claim the world's origin is the four composite natures. Note what they call them.
They call them elements (عناصر) or stoicheia (إسطقسات).
4
Grasp the shared doubt
The text states a common doubt (شبهة) shared by these eternalists. Identify it and ask why it leads them to deny creation from nothing.
The doubt: existence of a thing from nothing is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تبصرة الأدلة لأبي المعين النسفي ج
الناس نسبة هذا القول إليه وقالوا: إنه كان يقول بحدوث العالم وثبوت الصانع القديم، والمذهب المشهور عن جالينوس التوقف في القول بحدوث العالم وقدمه، وثبوت الصانع وعدمه، وفي ثبوت المعاد ونفيه. ومنهم من زعم أن أصل العالم الطبائع المركبة، وهي النار والماء والأرض والهواء ويسمونها العناصر، وقد يسمونها الإسطقسات. وشبهة هؤلاء كلهم أن وجود الشيء لا من شيء محال وكل صانع يصنع في محل إلا أن يوجد المحل أصلا.
هذه عمدة أقاويل هؤلاء، ولهم أقاويل مختلفة جارية مجرى الفروع لهذه فأعرضنا عن ذكرها لخروجها عما هو شرط كتابنا هذا، ونبين فساد هذه الأقاويل بعد إقامة الدليل على كون العالم بجميع أقسامه محدثا إن شاء الله تعالى.
The people attributed this saying to him and said: He used to affirm the temporal origination of the world and the existence of the eternal Maker. The famous doctrine attributed to Galen is suspension of judgment regarding the temporal origination and eternity of the world, the existence and non-existence of the Maker, and the affirmation and negation of the resurrection. And among them are those who claim that the origin of the world is the composite natures, which are fire, water, earth, and air; they call them the elements, and they sometimes call them the stoicheia. The doubt of all these is that the existence of a thing from nothing is impossible, and every maker makes in a substrate, unless the substrate exists originally. These are the main points of their sayings. They have various other sayings that are like branches of these, so we refrained from mentioning them because they are outside what is required for this book of ours. We will show the corruption of these sayings after establishing the proof that the world in all its parts is temporally originated, God willing.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a survey of the opinions of those who claim the world or parts of it are eternal. The author first mentions an unnamed person (perhaps Galen) to whom people attribute the belief in the world's temporality and the existence of an eternal Maker, though the famous view of Galen is suspension of judgment on these matters. Then he describes a group who believe the world's origin is the four composite natures (fire, water, earth, air), called elements (عناصر) or stoicheia (إسطقسات). Their common doubt (شبهة) is that something cannot come from nothing, and every maker requires a pre-existing substrate. The author states that these are the main arguments, and he will not mention their secondary branches. He then declares his intention to refute these views after establishing proof that the entire world is temporally originated (حادث). This sets the stage for the subsequent proof.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
صانع
عالم
طبائع
عناصر
إسطقسات
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Galen affirmed the world's creation, but the text says his famous view is suspension of judgment.
Correction: Galen neither affirms nor denies creation/eternity and the existence of the Maker.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the famous doctrine attributed to Galen?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world is temporally originated.
C) Suspension of judgment on several issues.
D) Reliance on the four elements.

Key Takeaway
Eternalists share the doubt that creation from nothing is impossible, but their specific theories differ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt the weight of the eternalist's doubt—that something cannot come from nothing? When you look at the world, does your heart ever waver in its certainty that Allah brought it into existence from absolute non-existence? What concrete step will you take this week to strengthen your conviction in creation ex nihilo?
?
Galen suspended judgment on whether the Maker exists. Are there areas in your life where you are suspending judgment on Allah's existence or attributes—perhaps acting as if He doesn't see, or neglecting to rely on His power? What would change if you fully believed that He is the Maker who creates without any pre-existing matter?
?
The elementalists claimed the world's origin is the elements, removing the need for a Creator. What 'elements' in your daily life—your wealth, health, abilities, or relationships—do you sometimes treat as self-sufficient origins rather than as signs of Allah's creation? How can you shift your perspective to see them as contingent and dependent on Him?
