The Regress of Description: Who Is Described by the Attributes?
Nasafi examines the logical problems of the essence being an attribute and the regress of description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second part of Nasafi's argument, which examines the logical consequences of considering the essence itself as an attribute (hypostasis) and raises the problem of infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the essence is an attribute, it must have a subject, leading to regress or contradiction.

After the initial dilemma, Nasafi presses further. He considers the Christian claim that the essence itself is one of the hypostases (the Father). This raises a critical question: if the essence is an attribute, what is the subject that it describes? Nasafi explores three possibilities, each leading to absurdity. Let's follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'ثم قيل لهم' to the end, along with its translation. Note the three sub-questions.
2
Identify the three options
Identify the three possible answers to the question: 'who is described by the attribute (the essence)?' The options are: 1) something other than essence, 2) the essence itself, 3) no subject.
The Arabic uses 'شيئا وراء الذات', 'هو الذات بعينه', 'صفة ولا موصوف له'.
3
Analyze each option's consequence
For each option, understand why it leads to a contradiction: option 1 makes God the subject and essence an attribute of another; option 2 leads to an infinite regress (attribute of attribute); option 3 affirms an attribute without a subject, which is unreasonable.
4
Recognize the rhetorical closure
Notice how Nasafi concludes by saying the imams have fully refuted these ideas and he suffices with this concise argument.
He aims to show the doctrine is false without lengthy elaboration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول لهم: أتزعمون أن العلم والحياة معنيان وراء الذات أم تزعمون أنهما يرجعان إلى الذات؟ فإن قالوا: هما راجعان إلى الذات فهو إذا جوهر واحد أقنوم واحد. وإن قالوا هما معنيان وراء الذات، قيل لهم: قولوا إنه جوهر واحد وله أقنومان ولا تقولوا ثلاثة أقانيم. ثم قيل لهم: إذا كان الجوهر الذي هو الذات من جملة الأقانيم الصفات فمن الموصوف به؟ فإن قالوا شيئا وراء الذات، فقد جعلوا الذات صفة لموصوف فإذا الله تعالى هو الموصوف لا هذا الجوهر الذي هو صفة لغيره، وإن قالوا: هو الذات بعينه، فقد جعلوا الذات صفة لنفسه موصوفا له، فإذا هو صفة صفته وموصوف موصوفه. وإن قالوا: هو صفة ولا موصوف له، فقد أثبتوا صفة لا لموصوف، وهذا باطل خارج عن قضية العقول. وقد أطنب أئمة أهل الإسلام في إيضاح مقالاتهم، وإبطال ذلك كله على وجه يتيقن المنصف بالوقوف عليها على التحاق القوم بأهل التجاهل ولا وجه إلى ذكر ذلك في كتابنا المبني على إيجاز، فاكتفينا بهذا القدر، والله الموفق.

Nasafi's regress argument demonstrates that the Christian conception of the essence as a hypostasis leads to an insoluble logical problem regarding the subject of description.

?

Extreme Anthropomorphist Views of Hisham ibn al-Hakam
Nasafi recounts corporealist claims of Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Muqatil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse corporealist claims attributed to early Islamic figures like Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Muqatil ibn Sulayman.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphist views varied wildly, from a pure ingot to a beardless youth.

Nasafi reports several extreme anthropomorphic descriptions of God that were circulated in early Islamic history. These descriptions, attributed to figures like Hisham ibn al-Hakam and Muqatil ibn Sulayman, illustrate the diversity of corporealist thought. The passage shows that such views were not uniform but ranged from a pure ingot to a being with human-like features.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analyzing. Understand the flow of the author's reporting.
2
Identify the groups and their claims
Notice the unnamed groups that said God has black hair from black light, or is like a beardless youth. Then note Hisham's three reported descriptions and Muqatil's agreement.
The claims are not consistent even within the same group.
3
Consider the significance of multiple descriptions
Reflect on the fact that Hisham changed his opinions multiple times, as recorded by al-Jahiz. This shows the instability of anthropomorphic views.
4
Understand the author's conclusion
Nasafi concludes that all these groups oppose the Ahl al-Sunna both in meaning and wording. This frames the report as a polemic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآدمي، حتى قال بعضهم: إن له شعرا أسود من نور أسود، وبعضهم قالوا: هو على صورة غلام أمرد له شعر جعد قطط. فأما هشام بن الحكم فالمحكي عنه أنه قال: إنه كالسبيكة الصافية يتلألأ، وروي عنه أنه قال: هو سبعة أشبار بشبر نفسه، وحكي أنه قيل له: هو أكبر أم هذا الجبل؟ وأشير إلى جبل كان بقربه، فقال: لا بل الجبل أعظم منه. وحكي الجاحظ عنه في كتابه الرد على المشبهة: أنه انتقل في سنة واحدة في الله تعالى إلى خمسة أقوال وذهب إلى مثل مذهب هؤلاء في التجسيم والتشبيه مقاتل بن سليمان صاحب التفسير هؤلاء كلهم يخالفوننا في المعنى واللفظ.
Concerning the human, until some of them said: He has black hair from black light, and some said: He is in the form of a beardless youth with curly hair. As for Hisham ibn al-Hakam, it is reported that he said: He is like a pure ingot that glitters, and it is reported from him that he said: He is seven spans of His own span. And it is related that he was asked: Is He greater or this mountain? And he pointed to a mountain near him, so he said: No, rather the mountain is greater than Him. And al-Jahiz reported from him in his book The Refutation of the Anthropomorphists that he changed in one year concerning Allah, the Exalted, to five opinions. And Muqatil ibn Sulayman, the author of the Tafsir, went to the like of the doctrine of these people in corporealism and anthropomorphism. All of these oppose us in meaning and word.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a report of extreme anthropomorphic views. It begins by mentioning unnamed groups who say God has black hair from black light, or is like a beardless youth. Then it focuses on Hisham ibn al-Hakam, who said God is like a pure ingot, seven spans of His own span, and that a mountain is greater than Him. It also notes that al-Jahiz recorded Hisham's multiple changes. Finally, Muqatil ibn Sulayman held similar views. The author concludes that all these groups oppose the Ahl al-Sunna both in meaning and in terminology.

Key Terms
تجسيم
تشبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: All anthropomorphists held identical views.
Correction: The passage shows a wide diversity of specific claims, from a pure ingot to a beardless youth.

This passage documents the diverse and contradictory anthropomorphic claims that the Ahl al-Sunna rejected.

?

?
The passage shows that even within anthropomorphism, there was great confusion. In your daily life, do you actively seek to protect your tawhid from subtle forms of likening Allah to creation (like in movies, books, or mental images)? What practical steps will you take?

The Karramiyya Definition of Body as Self-Subsistent
Nasafi explains Karramiyya's jism definition and their early affirmation of contact with the Throne.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Karramiyya's unique definition of "body" (jism) as self-subsistent, how they differed from Ahl al-Sunna only in wording, and why their early scholars affirmed composition while later ones disavowed it.

Core Idea
The Karramiyya called Allah a body but meant only that He is self-subsistent, not composed.

The Karramiyya sect presented a curious case: they used the same word "body" for Allah as anthropomorphists, but claimed they meant something entirely different. Nasafi clarifies their actual position, distinguishes their early and later scholars, and notes the crucial link between their view of divine contact with the Throne and their affirmation of composition. This lesson lays out these distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Nasafi presents the Karramiyya's position and then contrasts early vs later views.
Pay attention to the phrase 'القائم بالذات' – this is key.
2
Identify the verbal vs real disagreement
Nasafi says the disagreement is in name (ism) but not in meaning (ma'na). The Karramiyya deny composition and division. This is crucial to understand.
Hisham ibn al-Hakam's view is mentioned as similar – body = existent.
3
Distinguish early and later Karramiyya
Later Karramiyya disavow composition; early ones affirmed it because they believed Allah contacts the Throne. Note the logical consequence: contact requires a composite body.
The author speculates about later Karramiyya; important for historical nuance.
4
Reflect on the Throne contact argument
The early Karramiyya's belief that Allah is in contact (mumāss) with the Throne forced them to affirm composition. Nasafi uses this to show inconsistency.
Consider why contact implies composition: only a composite body can contact every part of another composite body.
5
Consider the author's method
Nasafi promises to refute both groups with truth. Notice his approach: he first clarifies positions before rebutting.
This is a typical kalam method: define then refute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطائفة الثانية هم الكرامية، وهم يخالفوننا في الاسم فيقولون: إنه جسم وساعدونا في الظاهر في المعنى فيقولون: لا نعني بقولنا إنه جسم أنه متبعض متجزئ متركب بل نريد أنه القائم بالذات. وحكي عن هشام بن الحكم أنه كان يقول الجسم عبارة عن الموجود. فكان الخلاف بين هؤلاء وبيننا في العبارة، وظني أن المتأخرين من الكرامية يتبرؤن عن إثبات التركيب لما ظهر لهم من فساد ذلك، فأما أوائلهم فقد كانوا يثبتون ذلك لأن الرواية عنهم ظاهرة أنه مماس للعرش، ولا شك أن العرش جسم متركب متبعض متجزئ لا يماس جميع صفحته العليا إلا الجسم المتركب يلاقي كل جزء منه جزء منه. ونتكلم مع كل واحد من الفريقين بما يحق الحق ويبطل الباطل ولو كره المجرمون.
The second group are the Karramiyya, and they oppose us in name, saying that He is a body, but they agree with us in the apparent meaning, saying: We do not mean by our saying that He is a body that He is composed of parts, divisible, or compound, but rather we mean that He is self-subsistent. And it is related from Hisham ibn al-Hakam that he used to say that 'body' is an expression for the existent. So the disagreement between these and us was in expression. And I think that the later Karramiyya disavow affirming composition because the corruption of that has become clear to them. As for their early ones, they used to affirm it because the report from them is clear that He is in contact with the Throne, and no doubt the Throne is a compound, divisible, composed body; only a compound body can contact all of its upper surface, each part of it meeting a part of Him. And we will speak with each of the two groups with what establishes the truth and nullifies falsehood, even if the criminals dislike it.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage defines the Karramiyya's position. They agree with Ahl al-Sunna that God is not composite, but they call Him a body meaning simply that He is self-subsistent (al-qa'im bi-dh-dhat). The author then mentions a similar view from Hisham, noting the disagreement was only verbal. Then the author speculates that later Karramiyya avoid affirming composition, but early ones did because they thought God contacts the Throne, which requires a composite body. The author promises to refute both groups.

Key Terms
كرامية
جسم
القائم بالذات
تركيب
مماس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Karramiyya are identical to other anthropomorphists who affirm a literal, composite body for Allah.
Correction: The Karramiyya explicitly deny that their use of 'jism' implies composition, parts, or division. They mean simply that Allah is self-subsistent (qa'im bi-dh-dhat), which Ahl al-Sunna also affirm. So the disagreement is primarily verbal, not theological.

Checkpoint

A) That He is self-subsistent
B) That He is composed of parts (tarkib)
C) That He exists
D) That He is living

Key Takeaway
The Karramiyya's claim that Allah is a 'body' does not necessarily imply anthropomorphism; they mean self-subsistence, but their early belief in contact with the Throne led them to affirm composition, which later Karramiyya rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the Karramiyya redefined 'body' to mean 'self-subsistent' in order to avoid a flawed understanding of Allah. Are there any beliefs you hold about Allah that you have redefined to make them comfortable, without fully confronting their implications? Will you examine them today with honesty?
?
The later Karramiyya abandoned their early position when they saw its corruption. Is there a belief about Allah or religion that you once held but now suspect might be flawed? What would it cost you to let it go, and are you willing to pay that price for the truth?
?
Nasafi says the disagreement between the Karramiyya and Ahl al-Sunna is only in wording, not in meaning. Think of a dispute you have had with another Muslim over a theological term. Was the disagreement really about the word, or about a deeper meaning? Can you take the first step today to separate terminology from substance and seek unity?

Scriptural Proofs of Anthropomorphists
Imam Nasafi enumerates scriptural proofs used by anthropomorphists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key scriptural proofs that anthropomorphists use to support their claim that God has physical attributes, as listed by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists rely on the literal wording of scriptures to argue for God's physicality.

Imam al-Nasafi begins his refutation of the anthropomorphists by first presenting their scriptural evidence. These verses and hadith, taken literally, appear to describe God with human-like body parts. In this lesson, we examine the specific citations the opponents cling to, as recorded by Imam al-Nasafi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the range of scriptural evidence—some are Quranic verses, others are hadith.
2
Identify the types of evidence
Categorize the proofs: which are verses about God's hands/eyes/foot/laugh, and which are about Adam's creation and charity?
3
Reflect on anthropomorphic interpretation
Think about how a literal reading leads to anthropomorphism. The author merely reports these; he does not interpret them here.
4
Understand the broader context
This passage serves as the starting point for Nasafi's subsequent refutation. Knowing these proofs helps you follow the theological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المخالفون لنا في الاسم والمعنى فهم يتعلقون بظواهر الآيات والأحاديث في نحو قوله عز وجل: خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَيَّ وَلِتُصْنَعَ عَلَى عَيْنِي وَالسَّمَاوَاتُ مَطْوِيَّاتٌ بِيَدَيْهِ وَبِيَمِينِهِ وَحَسْرَتَا عَلَى مَا فَرَّطْتُ فِي جَنْبِ اللَّهِ وَاللَّهُ نُورُ السَّمَاوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضِ والمعروف أنه خلق آدم على صورته، وأن الجبار يضع قدمه في النار، وأنه يضحك إلى أوليائه حتى تبدو نواجذه، وأن الصدقة تقع في كف الرحمن فيربيها أحدكم فَلُوَّه. في أمثال لهذا كثيرة.
As for those who oppose us in name and meaning, they cling to the apparent meanings of the verses and hadith such as His saying, Exalted is He: 'I created with My two hands' and 'that you be formed under My eye' and 'the heavens folded up in His right hand' and 'by His right hand' and 'Alas over what I neglected in the side of God' and 'God is the light of the heavens and the earth' and the known (hadith) that He created Adam upon His image, and that the Compeller places His foot in the Fire, and that He laughs at His friends until his molars appear, and that charity falls into the palm of the All-Merciful and He raises it as one of you raises his foal. And many similar things.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a listing of the scriptural proofs—verses and hadith—that the anthropomorphists (mujassimah) use to argue that God has a physical body. The author, Imam al-Nasafi, presents these as the starting point of the opponents' case. First, he cites Quranic verses: 'I created with My two hands' (Surah Sad 38:75), 'that you be formed under My eye' (Surah Ta-Ha 20:39), 'the heavens folded up in His right hand' (Surah al-Zumar 39:67), 'by His right hand' (Surah al-Zumar 39:67), 'Alas over what I neglected in the side of God' (Surah al-Zumar 39:56), and 'God is the light of the heavens and the earth' (Surah al-Nur 24:35). Then he mentions well-known hadith: that God created Adam upon His image (meaning Adam's image, not God's), that the Compeller (al-Jabbar) places His foot in the Fire (interpreted as a created being named 'foot' or metaphor for power), that He laughs at His friends until his molars appear, and that charity falls into the palm of the All-Merciful and He raises it. The passage concludes that these are just a few examples among many. The author does not discuss interpretation here; he simply reports what the opponents cling to.

Key Terms
شبه (shubuh)
ظواهر (zawahir)
آيات (ayat)
أحاديث (ahadith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These verses and hadith must be taken literally without interpretation.
Correction: Imam al-Nasafi is reporting the literalist position; the appropriate interpretation (ta'wil) is required to avoid anthropomorphism.

Checkpoint

B) 'God is the light of the heavens and the earth'
C) 'God is with you wherever you are'
D) 'He created Adam upon His image'

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphists use literal readings of Quranic verses and hadith to claim God has physical attributes, but these require proper interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
This passage shows that even Muslims can misunderstand God's attributes. How does your understanding of God's attributes affect your daily worship? Is there a disconnect between what you profess about God's transcendence and how you actually feel about Him in prayer? Make a concrete intention to reflect on God's otherness during your next prayer.

The Anthropomorphist Rational Argument for Divine Corporeality
Imam Nasafi presents the rational argument of anthropomorphists for God being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the anthropomorphist rational argument for God being a body and why it is based on analogy from the witnessed world.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists argue: every living being we know is a body, so God must be one too.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents the rational proof that certain groups use to claim that God is a body. The argument rests on an analogy from the created world to the unseen, a common sense intuition, and a threefold division of existents. Understanding this argument is crucial before studying its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage presents a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the three prongs of the argument
The argument has three stages: (1) analogy from shahid to gha'ib, (2) appeal to common intuition, (3) the threefold division of existents. Find each one in the text.
These stages build on each other.
3
Analyze the analogy from the witnessed world
Focus on the first part: how do the anthropomorphists use the attributes of God (living, seeing, etc.) to argue that He must be a body? What premise do they rely on?
Consider why they think the analogy is valid.
4
Examine the appeal to common sense
The second stage claims that even ordinary people intuitively know that only bodies can have these attributes. Why does this strengthen their case?
Think about the rhetorical force of this appeal.
5
Understand the threefold division of existents
The third stage uses a division of all existents into accidents, substances, and bodies. How does this lead to the conclusion that if God is not a body, He would be non-existent?
This is the most technical part of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتعلقون أيضا بما تراءى لهم أنه دليل معقول من ذلك أنه حي سميع بصير فاعل، وكل حي سميع بصير فاعل في الشاهد جسم، ويستحيل اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الأوصاف، وما يستحيل في الشاهد يستحيل في الغائب، ألا ترى أنه كما يستحيل في الشاهد أن يكون ما ليس عالما قادرا فاعلا، يستحيل مثله في الغائب، فكذا ها هنا كما يستحيل ثبوت هذه الأوصاف لما ليس بجسم في الشاهد يستحيل في الغائب، على أنه تقرر في بداية عقول العوام استحالة اتصاف ما ليس بجسم بهذه الصفات، حتى إن مخبرا لو أخبر أنه رأى حيا سمعيا بصيرا، لم يشك أحد أنه كان جسما، ولا يشتغل بالسؤال أنه كان جسما أو غير جسم، استغناء منه بما علم ببديهة عقله بكونه جسما، وما هذا سبيله ليس بإثبات مثله ممكنا لا في الشاهد ولا في الغائب، ولأنه تعالى ليس بعرض ولا جوهر لما مر، فلو لم يكن جسما لالتحق بالعدم لانحصار الموجودات على هذه الأقسام الثلاثة أعني أنها أعراض وجواهر وأجسام ولا يعقل موجود خارج عن هذه الأقسام، فلو خرج عن كونه جسما مع خروجه عن كونه عرضا وجوهرا لم يبق إلى إثباته طريق، فكان القول بإخراجه عن هذه الأقسام قولا بعدمه.
They also cling to what appears to them as a rational proof, such as that He is living, hearing, seeing, acting, and every living, hearing, seeing, acting being in the witnessed world is a body, and it is impossible for what is not a body to be described with these attributes, and whatever is impossible in the witnessed world is impossible in the unseen. Do you not see that just as it is impossible in the witnessed world for that which is not knowing, powerful, and acting to be so, its like is impossible in the unseen. So likewise here, just as it is impossible for these attributes to be established for what is not a body in the witnessed world, it is impossible in the unseen. Moreover, it is established in the beginning of the minds of the common people the impossibility of describing what is not a body with these attributes, such that if an informant said that he saw a living, hearing, seeing being, no one would doubt that it was a body, nor would he busy himself with asking whether it was a body or not, relying on what he knows intuitively about its being a body. And what is of this way, its like cannot be established either in the witnessed or the unseen. And because He, Exalted is He, is neither an accident nor a substance, as has passed, so if He were not a body, He would be annexed to non-existence because existents are confined to these three categories: namely, they are accidents, substances, and bodies, and no existent can be conceived outside these categories. So if He were to be removed from being a body along with being removed from being an accident and substance, no path would remain to proving Him, so saying that He is removed from these categories is a saying that He is non-existent.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the rational proof (dalil ma'qul) that the anthropomorphists use to argue that God must be a body. The argument unfolds in three stages. First, they draw an analogy from the witnessed world (al-shahid) to the unseen (al-gha'ib): in the witnessed world, every being that is living, hearing, seeing, and acting is a body (like humans). Therefore, God, who has these attributes, must also be a body. They claim that what is impossible in the witnessed world (e.g., a non-body having these attributes) is also impossible in the unseen. Second, they appeal to common sense (bida'at 'uqul al-'awamm): even ordinary people intuitively know that only bodies can have these attributes—if someone says they saw a living, hearing, seeing being, they immediately assume it is a body. Third, they use a division of existents (inqisam al-mawjudat): all existents fall into three categories—accidents (a'rad), substances (jawahir), and bodies (ajsam). God is neither an accident nor a substance (as established earlier), so if He is not a body, He would be non-existent ('adam). Thus, they conclude that denying that God is a body is equivalent to denying His existence. The author presents this argument without immediate refutation; the refutation follows in the next section.

Key Terms
شاهد (shahid)
غائب (gha'ib)
جسم (jism)
عرض ('arad)
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the analogy from shahid to gha'ib is always valid in all respects without considering divine transcendence.
Correction: The analogy from the witnessed world to God (the unseen) is not absolute because God is transcendent and not comparable to created beings. The attributes of God are not identical to human attributes in their modality.

Checkpoint
According to the rational argument of the anthropomorphists, why must God be a body?
A) Because He is described as having a hand and eye
B) Because every living, seeing, hearing being in the witnessed world is a body
C) Because God is composed of parts
D) Because the Quran commands belief in His corporeality

Key Takeaway
The anthropomorphist argument for divine corporeality relies on an analogy from the witnessed world, common intuition, and a threefold division of existents to claim that denying God's body is denying His existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously imagined God as having a form or shape, even while denying it with your tongue? What is in your heart right now about how you picture God when you pray? Will you today ask Him to purify your conception of Him from any likeness to creation?
?
The anthropomorphists trusted their intuition that only bodies can be living and hearing. What assumptions about God do you hold that come from your own experience rather than from revelation? How will you distinguish between what your mind expects of God and what He has told you about Himself?
?
If someone told you that denying God is a body leads to denying His existence (as the argument claims), would you feel pressured to accept their conclusion? Are you willing to affirm God's existence even when human categories fail to contain Him? Write down a commitment to trust God's transcendence over your own logic.

The Mu'tazili Definition of Similitude
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazili definition of similitude based on the most specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili criterion for similitude as sharing the most specific attribute (أخص وصف).

Core Idea
Similitude is about sharing what makes a thing unique, not general attributes.

The Mu'tazila, a major theological school, defined similitude in a precise way. Abu Hashim and Ibn al-Akhshid argued that two things are similar only if they share the most specific attribute, not general ones like existence or color. This passage explains their reasoning with the example of black and white.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the technical term 'أخص وصف'.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Identify the two proofs
Find where the author presents the first proof (example of black/white) and the second proof (correlation of similitude and difference).
Each proof supports the same definition.
3
Reflect on the criterion
Consider why general attributes like existence are insufficient for similitude according to the Mu'tazila.
This criterion distinguishes their view from others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلام المعتزلة في التماثل: وقال أبو هاشم وأبو بكر بن الأخشيد من جملة المعتزلة : إنهما المشتركان في أخص وصف، واستدلا بذلك أن لا مماثلة بين السواد والبياض مع اشتراكهما في الوجود وكونهما لونين وعرضين لما أن هذه أوصاف عامة، فلما جاء الاشتراك في كونهما سوادين وهو أخص أوصافهما ثبتت المماثلة، ولأن المماثلة بين المتماثلين تقع بما تقع به المخالفة بين غيرهما، والسواد يخالف البياض بكونه سوادا لا بكونه موجودا أو عرضا أو لونا دل أنه يماثل البياض بكونه بياضا.
The saying of the Mu'tazila concerning similitude: And Abu Hashim and Abu Bakr ibn al-Akhshid from the group of the Mu'tazila said: They (the two similars) are the two that share in the most specific attribute (akhass wasf). And they used as a proof for that that there is no similitude between blackness and whiteness despite their sharing in existence, being colors and accidents, because these are general attributes. So when the sharing in their being blacknesses occurs, which is their most specific attribute, similitude is established. And because similitude between two similars occurs by that by which difference occurs between others, and blackness differs from whiteness by being blackness, not by being existent, accident, or color, it indicates that it resembles whiteness by being whiteness.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazili definition of similitude (tamāthul) as formulated by Abu Hashim and Ibn al-Akhshid. They define two similars as those that share the most specific attribute (akhass wasf). They provide two proofs. The first proof uses the example of black and white: these two share general attributes (existence, being colors, accidents) but are not similars; only when they share the most specific attribute (being blacknesses) does similitude occur. The second proof argues that similitude and difference are correlated: since blackness differs from whiteness by its specific attribute (being black), it must also resemble it by its specific attribute (being white). Thus, the most specific attribute is the basis of similitude.

Key Terms
أخص وصف
تماثل
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila require two similars to be completely identical.
Correction: They only require sharing the most specific attribute, not all attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazili definition presented here, what is the criterion for two things to be similars?
A) They share all attributes
B) They share the most specific attribute
C) They can substitute for each other
D) They differ in only one attribute

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazili definition of similitude is sharing the most specific attribute, not general ones.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare yourself to other Muslims. On what basis? Is it their practice, knowledge, or something deeper? The Mu'tazila insist that true similitude lies in the most specific attribute. What is the single most specific attribute that defines your faith—the one without which you would not be Muslim? Are you sure you share that with those you consider like-minded, or are you content with generalities?
?
When you differ with a fellow believer, what do you focus on—differences in opinion or the core attribute that unites you? The passage teaches that difference and similitude arise from the same attribute. If you differ on a non-essential matter, do you still recognize the deep similitude in faith? Ask yourself: is there a relationship or community you have written off because of general differences, while the most specific attribute of faith is actually shared? What would it take to restore that bond?

Refutation of the Mu'tazili Definition of Similitude
Nasafi refutes both arguments of the Mu'tazili definition of similitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazili definition of similitude by exposing its logical flaws.

Core Idea
Similitude requires agreement in all attributes, not just the most specific.

The Mu'tazila defined similitude as sharing the most specific attribute. Nasafi responds with two arguments. The first shows that true similitude demands complete agreement, and the second exposes an absurd consequence regarding the eternal and originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the structure: two separate answers.
2
Identify the two arguments
The text begins with 'والجواب عن الأول' and then 'والجواب عن الثاني'. Distinguish the two refutations.
3
Analyze the first refutation
Understand why the Mu'tazili example of black and white fails. According to Nasafi, similitude requires sharing all attributes, not just the most specific.
This rejects the Mu'tazili premise entirely.
4
Analyze the second refutation
Examine the analogy with the eternal and originated. If the Mu'tazili criterion were true, it would force absurd conclusions about the eternal.
Note the parallel to the black/whiteness example.
5
Summarize the lesson
Reflect on how both arguments undermine the Mu'tazili definition and reinforce the Ash'ari/Maturidi understanding of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على المعتزلة: والجواب عن الأول : أن المماثلة ما كانت لاشتراكهما في كونهما سوادين بل لاستحالة اختصاص أحدهما بوصف يستحيل على الآخر، ولا ينفصل هو ممن يستدل عليه فيقول : لما لم تثبت المماثلة إلا باشتراكهما في جميع الأوصاف عامها وخاصها دل على أن لا مماثلة بدون هذا، وإنما يقع بالاشتراك فيها كلها لا في أخص الأوصاف. والجواب عن الثاني أن يقال لهم: ماذا تقولون؟ أن المحدث هل يخالف القديم بصفة الحدوث أم لا؟ فإن قالوا : لا وجب اشتراكهما في الحدوث حتى يكون القديم محدثا . كما أن السواد لما لم يخالف البياض بكونه لونا كانا لونين، وكذا بكونه عرضا () وموجودا.
The refutation of the Mu'tazila: And the answer to the first (argument): that similitude was not due to their sharing in being blacknesses, but rather due to the impossibility of one of them being characterized by a description that is impossible for the other. And he (the refuter) does not separate from the one who uses this as evidence, for he says: Since similitude is not established except by their sharing in all attributes, general and specific, it indicates that there is no similitude without this, and it occurs only by sharing in all of them, not in the most specific attributes. And the answer to the second (argument) is that it is said to them: What do you say? Does the originated (al-muhdath) differ from the eternal (al-qadīm) by the attribute of origination (al-ḥudūth) or not? For if they say: No, then their sharing in origination would be necessary, so that the eternal would be originated. Just as blackness, since it did not differ from whiteness by being a color, they were two colors, and likewise by being an accident and existent.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage contains the refutation of the Mu'tazili definition. It addresses both of their proofs in order. The first answer rejects the idea that similitude depends on the most specific attribute. Instead, it argues that similitude requires agreement in all attributes, both general and specific (jami' al-awsaf 'ammaha wa khassaha). The Mu'tazili example of black and white, when properly understood, shows that black and white are not similars because they differ in their most specific attributes; therefore, similitude actually requires complete agreement. The second answer uses an argument by analogy with the distinction between the originated (al-muhdath) and the eternal (al-qadim). If the Mu'tazili claim that difference is by the most specific attribute, then they must hold that the originated differs from the eternal by the attribute of origination. If they deny this, they would be forced to admit that the eternal shares in origination, which is absurd. The argument parallels the black/white example: just as blackness and whiteness share color because they do not differ by it, so too if the originated does not differ by origination, they would share it. The text cuts off, but the reasoning is clear.

Misconception: Thinking that the refutation accepts the Mu'tazili premise but modifies it.
Correction: The refutation actually rejects the Mu'tazili premise entirely, arguing that similitude requires complete agreement in all attributes, not just the most specific.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The Mu'tazila were intelligent and sincere, yet their definition collapsed. Are there theological beliefs you hold that you have never examined for logical consistency with the Quran and Sunnah? Will you commit today to studying the refutations of the Salaf against flawed arguments, so that your faith is built on solid ground?
?
Nasafi forces us to consider: if we accept that the originated and eternal are totally different, then any analogy between them is dangerous. How does this reality affect your worship? Do you truly worship a God who is completely unlike anything you know, or do you pray to a mental image shaped by creation? Ask Allah to purify your tawḥīd.

The Condition for True Similarity
Refutes the Mu'tazila claim that similarity is by the most specific attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true similarity in kalam requires sharing all attributes, general and specific, not just the most specific attribute.

Core Idea
Similarity is not about a single feature—it demands complete attribute sharing.

The Mu'tazila argued that similarity between two things is based on their most specific attribute. The Maturidi refutation shows that this is insufficient. Instead, true similarity requires sharing all attributes, both general and specific. This lesson unpacks that argument and its logical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Read the first refutation
Read the passage carefully to understand the Mu'tazila claim and the Maturidi response.
Focus on the argument about blackness.
2
Identify the condition for similarity
Note that similarity is not established by a single shared attribute but by sharing all attributes.
3
Understand the opponent's assertion
The passage includes the opponent's reasoning that similarity requires sharing all attributes, general and specific.
This reinforces the refutation.
4
Apply to the example
Consider how this applies to the example of black and white: they share 'color' but differ in specific attribute.
Sharing 'color' does not make them similar; they need to share all attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول : أن المماثلة ما كانت لاشتراكهما في كونهما سوادين بل لاستحالة اختصاص أحدهما بوصف يستحيل على الآخر، ولا ينفصل هو ممن يستدل عليه فيقول : لما لم تثبت المماثلة إلا باشتراكهما في جميع الأوصاف عامها وخاصها دل على أن لا مماثلة بدون هذا، وإنما يقع بالاشتراك فيها كلها لا في أخص الأوصاف. والجواب عن الثاني أن يقال لهم: ماذا تقولون؟ أن المحدث هل يخالف القديم بصفة الحدوث أم لا؟ فإن قالوا : لا وجب اشتراكهما في الحدوث حتى يكون القديم محدثا . كما أن السواد لما لم يخالف البياض بكونه لونا كانا لونين، وكذا بكونه عرضا وموجودا. وإن قالوا نعم وجب أن تقع بين كل مشتركين في صفة الحدوث مماثلة فتكون المتضادات كلها متماثلة لاشتراكها في صفة الحدوث، ولأن السواد والبياض كل واحد منهما يخالف الحمرة بأخص وصفه لنفسه وهو كون السواد سوادا والبياض بياضا، فيجب أن يكون السواد مع البياض مثلين لاشتراكهما في مخالفة الحمرة بأخص أوصاف أنفسهما، وهذا باطل.
The answer to the first [argument] is that similarity is not due to their sharing in being black, but rather due to the impossibility of one being characterized by an attribute impossible for the other. And he is not separate from the one who argues by saying: Since similarity is established only by their sharing in all attributes, general and specific, it indicates that there is no similarity without this, and it occurs only by sharing in all of them, not in the most specific attributes. The answer to the second [argument] is to say to them: What do you say? Does the originated thing differ from the eternal thing by the attribute of origination or not? If they say no, then they [the originated and eternal] must share in origination so that the eternal would be originated, just as black, since it did not differ from white by being a color, they are both colors, and likewise by being an accident and existent. And if they say yes, then similarity must occur between every two things that share the attribute of origination, so all opposites would be similar because they share the attribute of origination. And because black and white each one of them differs from red by its most specific attribute of itself—black being black and white being white—then black and white must be similar because they share in differing from red by their most specific attributes of themselves. And this is false.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage provides two refutations of the Mu'tazila arguments. The first refutation (against the claim that similarity is by the most specific attribute) argues that similarity actually requires sharing all attributes, both general and specific. The opponent asserts that since similarity is not established except by sharing all attributes, there is no similarity without that. The second refutation uses a reductio ad absurdum against the Mu'tazila's second argument. The refuter asks whether originated beings differ from the eternal by the attribute of origination (huduth). If they say no, then the eternal would have to share origination, making it originated—absurd. If they say yes, then all originated things (including opposites) would be similar because they share origination—again absurd. Furthermore, using the Mu'tazila's own principle, black and white both differ from red by their most specific attributes (blackness and whiteness), so they would be similar for sharing that difference, yet they are obviously not similar. Hence the Mu'tazila position is false.

Key Terms
مماثلة
الاشتراك في جميع الأوصاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The text only criticizes the Mu'tazila and doesn't give a positive condition for similarity.
Correction: The text explicitly states that similarity is established only by sharing all attributes, general and specific.

Checkpoint
What does the first refutation argue is the condition for true similarity?
A) Sharing only the most specific attribute
B) Sharing only general attributes
C) Sharing all attributes (general and specific)
D) Sharing nothing at all

Key Takeaway
True similarity requires sharing all attributes, both general and specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you judge others as 'similar to you' based on a single shared characteristic (like ethnicity, wealth, or a sin you both commit)? This lesson teaches that true similarity requires sharing all attributes. Does your assumption of similarity with others ignore deeper differences that matter to Allah? Will you ask Allah to help you see people as He sees them?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that similarity by the most specific attribute leads to anthropomorphism. But have you ever inadvertently attributed human limitations to Allah because you focused on a single attribute? How does the lesson's insistence on complete attribute sharing remind you of God's absolute uniqueness? What will you do to purify your conception of Allah?

The Reductio on Origination and Similarity
Refutes the Mu'tazila using a dilemma about origination and attribute sharing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi refutes the Mu'tazila argument using a reductio ad absurdum concerning the attribute of origination.

Core Idea
If origination distinguishes the created from the eternal, then all created things would be similar—an absurdity.

The second Mu'tazila argument claimed that similarity is based on the most specific attribute of difference. The Maturidi refutation turns this principle against them by examining the attribute of origination. Through a clever dilemma, the refutation shows that the Mu'tazila position leads to contradictions, proving it false.

Guided Path
1
Read the second refutation
Read the passage carefully, focusing on the dilemma posed to the Mu'tazila.
The dilemma revolves around the attribute of origination (huduth).
2
Analyze the first horn
If the Mu'tazila say originated does not differ from eternal by origination, then the eternal must share origination, making it originated—absurd.
This horn leads to a contradiction.
3
Analyze the second horn
If they say originated does differ by origination, then all originated things share that attribute, so they would all be similar, including opposites—absurd.
This horn leads to an absurd consequence.
4
Consider the additional reductio
The passage then applies the Mu'tazila's own principle to colors: black and white both differ from red by their own specific attributes, so they should be similar, but they are not.
This further refutes the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الأول : أن المماثلة ما كانت لاشتراكهما في كونهما سوادين بل لاستحالة اختصاص أحدهما بوصف يستحيل على الآخر، ولا ينفصل هو ممن يستدل عليه فيقول : لما لم تثبت المماثلة إلا باشتراكهما في جميع الأوصاف عامها وخاصها دل على أن لا مماثلة بدون هذا، وإنما يقع بالاشتراك فيها كلها لا في أخص الأوصاف. والجواب عن الثاني أن يقال لهم: ماذا تقولون؟ أن المحدث هل يخالف القديم بصفة الحدوث أم لا؟ فإن قالوا : لا وجب اشتراكهما في الحدوث حتى يكون القديم محدثا . كما أن السواد لما لم يخالف البياض بكونه لونا كانا لونين، وكذا بكونه عرضا وموجودا. وإن قالوا نعم وجب أن تقع بين كل مشتركين في صفة الحدوث مماثلة فتكون المتضادات كلها متماثلة لاشتراكها في صفة الحدوث، ولأن السواد والبياض كل واحد منهما يخالف الحمرة بأخص وصفه لنفسه وهو كون السواد سوادا والبياض بياضا، فيجب أن يكون السواد مع البياض مثلين لاشتراكهما في مخالفة الحمرة بأخص أوصاف أنفسهما، وهذا باطل.
The answer to the first [argument] is that similarity is not due to their sharing in being black, but rather due to the impossibility of one being characterized by an attribute impossible for the other. And this is false.

The opponent asserts that since similarity is not established except by sharing all attributes, there is no similarity without that. Jubba'i's Definition of Similarity and Its Critique
Exposes inconsistency in al-Jubba'i's definition of similarity as sharing a self-attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's definition of similarity as sharing a self-attribute and why the author considers it corrupt due to its implications about causation and motion.

Core Idea
Defining similarity by self-attribute alone leads to an invalid causal explanation.

Al-Jubba'i's followers claimed that similars are those sharing a self-attribute, but this definition has problems. The author compares it to Abu Hashim's view, revealing terminological differences. More importantly, the definition relies on an invalid way of explaining motion without a cause, which the author critiques. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; we only have this portion.
2
Identify the two definitions of similarity
Find how Jubba'i defines similarity (shared self-attribute) and how Abu Hashim defines it (being black, etc.). Note the terminological difference.
3
Understand the problem with 'self-attribute'
Jubba'i's usage implies that only blackness is self-attribute, while Abu Hashim's would make all existents similar. Grasp why restriction to 'most specific' is needed.
4
Grasp the corruption argument
The author says Jubba'i's view leads to an invalid explanation of motion (not by itself nor by a meaning). Understand why this is considered 'explanation by the unintelligent'.
5
Connect to broader kalam principles
Reflect on why causation matters: everything must have a cause either in itself or externally. This principle underlies the critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم بعض أصحاب الجبائي أن المتماثلين هما المشتركان في الصفة النفسية وهذا هو عين مذهب أبي هاشم أن المماثلة بين السوادين تقع بكونهما سوادين لا بما وراء ذلك من الأوصاف إلا أن العبارة اختلفت لأن من مذهب الجبائي أن الموجود لا يقال له موجود لنفسه بل يقال : هو موجود لا لنفسه ولا لمعنى، وكذا العرض واللون، فأما السواد فهو سواد لنفسه فإذا قال: هما المشتركان في الصفة النفسية ينصرف ذلك إلى كونهما سوادين لا إلى كونهما عرضين أو لونين أو موجودين وأبو هاشم يقال : كما أن السواد سواد لذاته فكذا الموجود موجود لذاته، وكذا العرض، واللون فلو قال: هما المشتركان في الصفة النفسية أدى ذلك إلى كون كل موجودين مثلين، وكذا هذا في العرضين واللونين فقيد ذلك بأخص صفة نفسية، ثم كلام الجبائي فاسد لما مر في باب إثبات الأعراض أن لا يجوز أن يقال الشيء متحرك لا لنفسه ولا لمعنى، لأن هذا تعليل بالعُجمي إذ لابد أن يكون كونه متحركا إما لنفسه وإما لمعنى وراء نفسه، وهذا لما ذكرنا
And some of Jubba'i's followers claimed that similars are those that share the self-attribute (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). And this is exactly the doctrine of Abu Hashim that similarity between two black things occurs by their being black, not by attributes beyond that; except the expression differed. Because according to Jubba'i's doctrine, the existent is not said to be existent by itself, but rather it is said: it is existent not by itself and not by a meaning. And likewise the accident and the color. As for black, it is black by itself. So when he says 'the two share the self-attribute', that refers to their being black, not to their being accidents, colors, or existents. And Abu Hashim is said: Just as black is black by its essence, so the existent is existent by its essence, and likewise the accident and the color. So if he said 'the two share the self-attribute', that would lead to every two existents being similar, and likewise with two accidents and two colors. So he restricted it to the most specific self-attribute. Then the statement of Jubba'i is corrupt because of what has passed in the chapter on establishing accidents: that it is not permissible to say 'a thing moves not by itself and not by a meaning', because this is an explanation by the unintelligent (al-ʿujmī), since it must be that its moving is either by itself or by a meaning beyond itself. And this is what we mentioned.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage critiques the view of some followers of al-Jubba'i regarding similarity (tamathul). They define similars as those sharing the 'self-attribute' (al-sifat al-nafsiyya). The author notes this is essentially the same as Abu Hashim's view that similarity between two black things is by being black, not by other attributes. The difference is terminological: Jubba'i denies that existent, accident, or color are said to be by themselves; only black is black by itself. So 'self-attribute' for Jubba'i refers specifically to blackness. Abu Hashim, however, holds that existent is existent by itself, etc., so if he used 'self-attribute' it would wrongly imply all existents are similar. Thus Abu Hashim restricts to the most specific self-attribute. Then the author declares Jubba'i's statement corrupt, invoking an earlier argument against explaining motion without a cause: saying 'a thing moves not by itself and not by a meaning' is an invalid explanation (taʿlīl bi-l-ʿujmī - unintelligent reasoning). The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is that Jubba'i's position is inconsistent.

Key Terms
صفة نفسية
أخص صفة نفسية
تعليل بالعجمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing any self-attribute is sufficient for two things to be similar.
Correction: Only the most specific self-attribute (e.g., blackness) can define similarity; otherwise, all existents or accidents would be similar.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider Jubba'i's statement corrupt?
A) Because it denies that similarity exists at all
B) Because it uses an invalid explanation of motion without a cause
C) Because it contradicts the Mu'tazila entirely
D) Because it claims all existents are similar

Key Takeaway
Jubba'i's definition of similarity is rejected because it leads to an invalid causal explanation of motion, contradicting the principle that all motion has a cause either in itself or externally.

Refutation of the Ash'ari view on absolute similarity
Al-Nasafi critiques the Ash'ari position on similarity with linguistic and legal examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Nasafi refutes the Ash'ari claim that similarity requires equality in all respects, using linguistic and legal examples.

Core Idea
Similarity can be partial: two things can be alike in one respect yet different in others.

This passage from al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla directly engages with the Ash'ari school's strict definition of similarity (mumathala). The Ash'aris argued that similarity implies complete equality in all aspects, making it impossible to say anything is like another if any difference exists. Al-Nasafi rejects this, showing that ordinary language and Islamic law routinely accept partial similarity. The lesson examines his key arguments and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Try to identify the main claim al-Nasafi is rejecting.
Notice how the passage begins with 'ثم اعلم' indicating an important point.
2
Identify the Ash'ari claim
Locate the phrase 'إن لا مماثلة إلا بالمساواة من جميع الوجوه'. This is the Ash'ari position that similarity only exists with full equality.
The Ash'aris deny similarity when any difference exists.
3
Examine al-Nasafi's counterexamples
Al-Nasafi gives several examples from language, jurisprudence, and everyday life where partial similarity is used. Note each one and how they illustrate his point.
Examples include 'Zayd is like 'Amr', legal analogies, and the sultan replacing a guard.
4
Reflect on the hadith evidence
The hadith 'Wheat for wheat, like for like' shows that similarity in one aspect (measure) is accepted even if other aspects differ. This supports the Maturidi view.
The hadith is cited as a proof from Islamic law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم بأنا لا نقول ما تقول الأشعرية: إن لا مماثلة إلا بالمساواة من جميع الوجوه، وأن لا ثبوت لها مع تمكن المخالفة من وجه لتنافيها، بل نقول: يجوز أن يكون شيء مماثلا لشيء من وجه مخالفا له من وجه، فإنا نجد أهل اللغة لا يمنعون من القول بأن زيدا مثل لعمرو، وفي الفقه إذا كان يساويه فيه ويسد مسده في ذلك الباب وإن كان بينهما مخالفة لوجوه كثيرة، وكذا في الطول والقصر والحسن والدمامة والشجاعة والجبن، وغير ذلك حتى إن السلطان لو ولى رجلا لحماية ثغر ثم إنه عزله بمن يقوم مقامه ويسد مسده في الحماية والقدرة على دفع الأعداء لا يمتنع أهل اللغة أن يقولوا: عزل السلطان فلانا بمن هو مثله، وإن كانت بينهما مخالفة في أسباب كثيرة وصفات جمة، وكذا قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: 'الحنطة بالحنطة مثلا بمثل' وأراد به الاستواء في الكيل دون الوزن وعدد الحبات والصلابة والرخاوة، وأشباه ذلك.
Then know that we do not say what the Ash'ariyya say: that no similarity exists except by equality in all respects, and that it has no affirmation while the possibility of difference in one respect exists because they contradict. Rather we say: It is permissible for something to be similar to something in one respect and different from it in another respect. For we find that the people of language do not prevent the statement that Zayd is like 'Amr, and in jurisprudence if he equals him in a matter and fills his place in that case even if there are differences in many respects. Likewise in length and shortness, beauty and ugliness, bravery and cowardice, and other things. Even if a sultan appoints a man to protect a frontier and then replaces him with someone who takes his position and fills his place in protection and ability to repel enemies, the people of language do not refrain from saying: 'The sultan replaced so-and-so with someone who is his like,' even if there are differences in many causes and numerous attributes. And likewise the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم said: 'Wheat for wheat, like for like,' and he intended thereby equality in measure, not in weight, number of grains, hardness, softness, and the like.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Ash'ari position that similarity (mumathala) only occurs when two things are equal in all respects, and that if there is any difference, similarity is impossible. Al-Nasafi asserts the Maturidi view that similarity can be partial: something can be similar to another thing in one respect while differing in another. He supports this with multiple examples from common Arabic usage: saying 'Zayd is like 'Amr' does not imply full equality; in jurisprudence, one may be like another in a specific ruling even if they differ in other matters. Other examples include length and shortness, beauty and ugliness, bravery and cowardice. He provides the concrete scenario of a sultan replacing a frontier guard with someone else: people would say the new guard is 'like' the former even though they differ in many personal characteristics. Finally, he cites a hadith about selling wheat for wheat 'like for like,' which is understood to mean equality in measure, not in weight, number of grains, hardness, etc. This demonstrates that partial similarity is recognized in both language and Islamic law. Thus, the Ash'ari claim is too strict and not supported by actual usage.

Key Terms
مماثلة
مساواة
مخالفة
أشعرية
أهل اللغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari position that similarity requires total equality is the only correct one.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that partial similarity is accepted in language, law, and prophetic tradition, so the Ash'ari position is too restrictive.

Checkpoint
How does al-Nasafi refute the Ash'ari claim that similarity requires equality in all respects?
A) By arguing that similarity is impossible altogether.
B) By giving examples where partial similarity is accepted in language and law.
C) By denying that God has any similarity to creation.
D) By quoting a hadith that forbids describing anything as similar.

Key Takeaway
Similarity in kalam can be partial; it does not require absolute equality, as shown by language, law, and prophetic tradition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate another person's character, do you unconsciously demand that they match you in every way before you consider them 'like you'? How can this lesson's principle of partial similarity free you from unfair comparisons and deepen your relationships? This week, catch yourself when you dismiss someone as 'completely different' and identify one area where you are actually similar.
?
Think about a judgment you recently made about someone's competence or worth. Did you require more similarity than was necessary, perhaps overlooking their strengths because they didn't fit your full ideal? How could the principle of partial similarity change how you treat that person starting today? Commit to acknowledging one positive trait they have that you share.
?
The lesson shows that even the Prophet allowed partial similarity in trade. How do you handle situations where people fall short of your expectations? Are you prone to rejecting them entirely because they differ in some ways? How can you apply the Maturidi approach of recognizing partial similarity while still acknowledging differences, especially in important relationships or community roles?

Al-Nashī's Fourfold Division of Shared Names
Nasafi presents al-Nashī's claim and his fourfold division, then dismisses it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nashī's claim that shared names imply similarity, his fourfold division of how things share names, and why Nasafi considers his argument flawed.

Core Idea
Shared names do not require similarity, and forcing a false dichotomy leads to error.

In this passage, Nasafi introduces and refutes the position of al-Nashī, a Mu'tazili theologian, who argued that if two things share a name univocally, they must be similar. Al-Nashī presents a fourfold classification of how names can be shared, which he uses to demand that the names applied to God and creatures must be metaphorical in one and literal in the other. Nasafi states that this argument is false, and by understanding it we can sharpen our grasp of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nashī's Claim and His Fourfold Division
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the four categories and the examples al-Nashī gives for each.
Pay attention to the types: resemblance, relation, one literal and one metaphorical, and the case where none apply.
2
Analyze Al-Nashī's Argument
See how al-Nashī uses the absence of the first three categories to force a conclusion: the names shared between God and creatures must be metaphorical for one side and literal for the other. Understand the dilemma he creates.
Consider the two narrations: which side is literal and which metaphorical?
3
Consider Nasafi's Dismissal
Nasafi opens by saying the falsity of this position is known 'by pausing at this statement'. Reflect on why he considers it flawed: the names of God are not like ordinary shared names; they are properly predicated of both without creating similarity.
Nasafi's refutation likely rests on the unique nature of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالوقوف على هذه الجملة يعرف بطلان قول عبد الله بن محمد المعروف بالناشي صاحب المقالات في ادعائه أن اشتراك مسميين في اسم واحد ينطلق عليهما على الحقيقة يوجب مماثلة بينهما ، فأصل لنفسه أصلا بأن قال: إن الشيئين إذا اشتركا في اسم واحد لم يخل القول في ذلك من أربعة أوجه إما أن يشتركا فيه لاشتباههما كاشتراك السوادين في اسم السواد إذ الاشتباه ما حملته ذاتهما من المعاني كالأسودين، أو لأجل مضاف أضيف إليه كأخوي رجل وابنيه، أو يكون الاسم حقيقة في أحدهما مجازا في الآخر كالأسد على الحيوان الذي له زئيره، وعلى الشجاع من البشر ، فإذا لم يوجد شيء من هذا الأقسام في قولنا: إنه تعالى شيء موجود حي عالم قادر لابد أن يجعل الأسماء مجازا في محل، حقيقة في المحل الآخر ، ثم اختلفت الروايات عنه في تعيين محل المجاز والحقيقة فقال في رواية هذه الأسامي الله تعالى حقيقة ولغيره مجازا، وقال في رواية: هي الله تعالى مجازا ولغيره حقيقة
And by pausing at this statement, one knows the falsity of the statement of Abdullah ibn Muhammad known as al-Nashi, the author of al-Maqalat, in his claim that the sharing of two named things in one name that applies to both truly necessitates similarity between them. So he established a principle for himself by saying: If two things share a single name, the statement about that is not free from four aspects: either they share it due to their resemblance, like the sharing of two blacks in the name 'blackness', for resemblance is what their essences carry of meanings like the two black things; or on account of an added thing added to it, like two brothers of a man and his two sons; or the name is literal in one and metaphorical in the other, like 'lion' for the animal that has a roar, and for the courageous human. So if none of these divisions exist in our statement that He, exalted, is a thing, existing, living, knowing, powerful, it is necessary to make the names metaphorical in one locus and literal in the other. Then the reports differ about him in determining the locus of metaphor and literalness: he said in one narration: these names are literal for God and metaphorical for others; and he said in another narration: they are metaphorical for God and literal for others.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of Abdallah ibn Muhammad al-Nashi', a Mu'tazili theologian, and his argument about the implications of sharing a name. The author first states that by understanding the previous argument, one recognizes the falsity of al-Nashi's claim that two things sharing a single univocal name necessitates similarity between them. Al-Nashi then establishes a principle: when two things share a name, there are exactly four possibilities. First, they share it because of resemblance (ishtibah), like two black things sharing the name 'blackness' due to a common quality. Second, because of an additional relation, like 'brother of a man' and 'son of a man' sharing a derived term. Third, the name is literal in one and metaphorical in the other, like 'lion' for the animal and for a brave human. Al-Nashi argues that if none of these three categories apply to the names used for God (like 'thing', 'existing', 'living', 'knowing', 'powerful'), then one must hold that the names are metaphorical for one subject and literal for the other. He is uncertain which: some reports say he held the names are literal for God and metaphorical for creatures; other reports say the opposite. This forces a dilemma: if the names are literal for God, creatures are only so metaphorically; if literal for creatures, God is only so metaphorically. The passage sets up al-Nashi's position for refutation.

Key Terms
الناشي (al-Nashi')
الاشتراك (sharing of names)
الحقيقة (literal meaning)
المجاز (metaphorical meaning)
الأوجه (aspects)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if two things share a name, they must be similar in all respects.
Correction: Al-Nashī himself lists three ways they can share a name without similarity (resemblance, relation, and literal/metaphorical), though he wrongly insists that if none apply, a forced similarity follows. In truth, divine names are not subject to this dichotomy.

Checkpoint
How many possible ways does al-Nashī list for two things sharing a single name?
A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Two

Key Takeaway
Al-Nashī's argument that shared names imply similarity is flawed and his fourfold division does not apply to the names of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's names, are you sometimes tempted to reduce them to mere human categories, putting God in a box? In what specific way has this lesson confronted that tendency in you today?
?
Al-Nashī's argument led to two contradictory conclusions. How often do you find yourself insisting on a logical system for understanding God, only to realize your own confusion? What will you do to remain humble before God's transcendence?
?
The lesson says al-Nashī's claim is 'false' from the start. Is there an area of your faith where you are clinging to a neat system of your own making, instead of submitting to God as He reveals Himself? What step can you take today to surrender that system?

The Inconsistency of Denying 'Thing' for God
Nasafi interrogates sects rejecting 'thing' for God

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying that God is a 'thing' leads to logical contradictions and how Nasafi exposes the inconsistency of the Jahmiyya, Bāṭiniyya, and philosophers.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'thing' does not imply similarity; it affirms existence.

This lesson presents a powerful dialectical argument from Imam al-Nasafi against those who refuse to apply the word 'thing' to Allah. By a series of simple questions, he forces the opposition into self-contradiction or absurdity. The passage demonstrates the precision of kalam reasoning and the importance of linguistic clarity in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythmic questioning come through before you interpret.
Notice the repetitive structure: 'If they say... it is said...'
2
Identify the main question stages
The argument proceeds through a series of conditional questions. First, is the word 'thing' meaningful or meaningless? Then, does it have a meaning? Then, what is that meaning? Trace each branch.
3
Analyze the dilemma about 'existent'
When the opponents say 'thing' means 'existent', Nasafi asks whether God is self-subsistent. Denying that leads to nihilism; affirming it leads to the question of similarity, trapping the opponent.
This shows that the refusal to call God a 'thing' ultimately leads to either ta'til or tashbih.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this argument shows that theological language must be used consistently and that rejecting a name for God can have harmful consequences.
The passage cuts off, but the direction is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال للجهمية، والباطنية والمتفلسفة الذين زعموا أن الله تعالى ليس بشيء: ماذا تزعمون أن لفظة الشيء مهملة أم مستعملة ؟ فإن قالوا: هي مهملة. ظهر بهتهم. وإن قالوا: هي مستعملة قيل: هل هي لها معنى أم لا؟ فإن قالوا: لا. فقد جمع أهل اللغة إلى السفه باستعمالهم لفظا لا طائل تحته. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: ما معناه؟ فإن قالوا: معناه العرض أو الجوهر أو الجسم أو أي معنى ادعوا بأن كذبهم باستعمالهم لفظة الشيء فيما وراء ذلك، فإنهم كما يسمون العرض شيئا يسمون الجسم والجوهر والحيوان والنبات والجماد وكل نوع من أنواع الموجودات شيئا. وإن قالوا: معناه أنه موجود قيل: هل الله تعالى ثابت الذات؟ فإن قالوا: لا. بان تعطيلهم. وإن قالوا: نعم. قيل: إذا كان ثابت الذات هل كانت ذات شبيها بغيره، وهل هو مثل لغيره فإن قالوا: نعم. شبهوه، وهم امتنعوا عن إطلاق هذا الاسم عليه فرارا من التشبيه وإن قالوا: لا. قيل : إذا لم تكن ذاته مثلا
Then it is said to the Jahmiyya, the Bāṭiniyya, and the philosophers who claimed that God Almighty is not a thing: What do you claim—is the word 'thing' meaningless or used? If they say: It is meaningless, their falsehood becomes evident. And if they say: It is used, it is said: Does it have a meaning or not? If they say: No, then they have attributed all the people of language to foolishness by their use of a word that has no benefit under it. And if they say: Yes, it is said: What is its meaning? If they say: Its meaning is accident, or substance, or body, or any meaning they claim, they refute themselves by their use of the word 'thing' for what is beyond that, for just as they call an accident a thing, they call the body, the substance, the animal, the plant, the inanimate, and every type of existent thing a thing. And if they say: Its meaning is that it is existent, it is said: Is God Almighty self-subsistent? If they say: No, their nihilism becomes clear. And if they say: Yes, it is said: If He is self-subsistent, is His essence similar to another, and is He like another? If they say: Yes, they have likened Him, but they refrained from applying this name to Him out of escape from anthropomorphism. And if they say: No, it is said: If His essence is not similar... [text cuts off]
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical refutation of three groups—Jahmiyya, Bāṭiniyya, and philosophers (mutafalsifa)—who deny that God can be called a 'thing' (shay'). Nasafi employs a series of logical questions to expose their inconsistency. He begins by asking whether the word 'thing' is used meaningfully or is meaningless. If they say it is meaningless, their position is exposed as false (buhtān). If they say it is used, the next question is whether it has a meaning. Answering 'no' would accuse all Arabic speakers of foolishness. If they say 'yes', then the meaning must be specified. If they propose meanings like 'accident' ('araḍ), 'substance' (jawhar), or 'body' (jism), Nasafi points out that they themselves use 'thing' for a much wider range of entities (animals, plants, etc.), so their narrow definition is contradicted by actual linguistic practice. If they say it means 'existent' (mawjūd), he then asks whether God is self-subsistent (thābit al-dhāt). Denying this would entail nihilism (ta'tīl). Affirming it, he then asks whether God's essence is similar to anything; answering 'yes' would be the very anthropomorphism they sought to avoid, while answering 'no' would show that calling God a 'thing' does not entail similarity. The passage is cut off mid-sentence, but the argument is clear: the refusal to apply 'thing' to God is either inconsistent or leads to indefensible consequences.

Key Terms
جهمية (Jahmiyya)
باطنية (Bāṭiniyya)
متفلسفة (mutafalsifa)
شيء (shay')
عرض ('araḍ)
جوهر (jawhar)
جسم (jism)
تعطيل (ta'tīl)
تشبيه (tashbīh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling God a 'thing' necessarily implies comparison to other things.
Correction: The term 'thing' is broad and does not force similarity; it affirms existence without tashbih.

Checkpoint
What is the first question Nasafi poses to those who deny that God is a 'thing'?
A) Is the word 'thing' meaningful or meaningless?
B) Is God similar to other things?
C) Is the word 'thing' used for accidents only?
D) Is God self-subsistent?

Key Takeaway
Denying God the name 'thing' leads to either linguistic absurdity, ta'til, or tashbih.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call God a 'thing', do you feel a hidden unease that you are reducing Him to the level of creation? Where does that feeling come from—reverence or an assumption that God must be so different that even existence cannot be common? Let this lesson confront your heart: are you trusting the language God Himself used in the Qur'an (e.g., 'God is the Creator of all things') or your own fear of similarity? Commit to using the term 'thing' for God without apology, knowing that existence does not imply likeness.
?
Have you ever avoided describing God with a specific attribute or name because it felt too 'ordinary' or 'human'? Examine your conscience: is there a part of your belief where you prefer silence over what Allah has called Himself, hoping to be more 'safe'? This lesson shows that such silence can lead to deeper errors. Will you renounce your own caution and submit to revelatory language, even when it feels uncomfortable?
?
The Jahmiyya thought they were defending God's transcendence; instead, their position collapsed into contradiction or worse. Is there an area of your belief where you have adopted a 'safe' position that actually contradicts clear evidence? Ask yourself: am I more concerned with appearing intellectually sophisticated or with truth? Let this lesson push you to examine the foundations of your convictions—not just on this issue, but on any issue where you might be tempted to go beyond what Allah has said.
